IMPLEMENTATION COMPLETION REPORT
PAKISTAN
FOURTH HIGHWAY PROJECT
(LN.28 14-PAK)
Preface
1.    This is the Implementation Completion Report (ICR) for the Fourth Highway Project in
Pakistan, for which Ln.2814-PAK in the amount of US$ 152 million was approved on
September 10, 1987 and made effective on November 16, 1987.
2.    The loan was closed on December 31, 1995, after a one year extension. It was fully
disbursed, and the last disbursement took place in May, 1996.
3.    The ICR was prepared by Hasan Masood, Consultant, under the supervision of
Navaid A. Qureshi, Projects Advisor, SA1PK; and reviewed by Marie Robinson, Chief,
SAIIN; and John Tillman, Sr. Economist; and Antonio J. Cittati, Sr. Highway Engineer,
SAlIN. The borrower has prepared its own evaluation report on the project, which is included
as an appendix to the ICR.
4.    Preparation of this ICR was initiated during the Bank's final supervision/completion
mission in October 1995. It is based on material on the project file, periodic management
reports prepared by the borrower and several technical and end-of-assignment reports prepared
by the consultants who worked on this project. The Borrower contributed to preparation of
the ICR by preparing its own evaluation of the project implementation, and reviewing the draft
ICR.






2
FOURTH HIGHWAY PROJECT
(LN.28 14-PAK)
PAKISTAN
Evaluation Summary
Introduction and Project Objectives
1.    The principal objectives of the project were to: (a) lower transport costs on the
Karachi - Peshawar N-5 Highway by road rehabilitation; (b) develop the capacity of NHB
(now NHA) to plan and manage highway maintenance and operation, and to introduce modern
technical and financial management practices; (c) improve traffic safety on National Highways;
(d) develop the capacity of Pakistani contractors to execute maintenance and rehabilitation
works using modem techniques, equipment and materials; and (e) continue to mobilize
revenues from road users for the maintenance of the National Highways.
2.    These objectives were to be achieved through: (i) rehabilitation of about 650 km of the
N-5 Highway and associated bridges; (ii) improvements to maintenance and traffic engineering
on National Highway; (iii) establishment of a pilot-scale highway patrol; (iv) expansion of the
national driver instructor training school; (v) institutional improvements in Balochistan C&W
Department; (vi) studies for future highway and road transportation projects; and (vii) training
and technical assistance.
3.    Most of the project components were reasonably well linked to the project objectives,
relevant to the country's needs and consistent with the Bank's strategy in the transport sector.
Implementation Experience and Results
4.    The physical objectives of the project were substantially achieved. The project was
completed within one year of the original target and the entire loan was fully disbursed.
Various sections of N-5 totaling 504 km were improved or dualized resulting in significant
savings in the vehicle operating costs. The project has a economic rate of return of 30.5%.
The institutional objectives in terms of improved management and technical and financial
reforms were largely met. NHA is presently managing a major program of large highway
construction and improvement contracts; has acquired technical expertise in modern
construction materials and methods; and is in the process of adopting streamlined
administrative policies and procedures, contract administration and financial management
practices. A seven year highway maintenance and implementation program was successfully
carried out by NHA primarily under the supervision of expatriate consultants. The program
was gradually handed over to the NHA staff towards the end of the project. This component
evolved from something small into a major achievement with lasting institutional impact and
spawned the Transport Sector Project. With the establishment of a separate maintenance
structure as a result of the project, highway maintenance activities have assumed an important



3
role within NHA. In addition, the creation of a traffic engineering unit to complement the
maintenance functions provided NHA in-house capacity to address traffic safety issues. The
Highway Patrol and Driver Instructor School components could not be implemented.
However, traffic safety was improved through a program of road signing, line marking, median
barrier construction and "black-spot" removal on the N-5. The project provided highly
significant benefits to the local construction industry, and a large number of Pakistani
contractors are now capable of undertaking highway improvement and maintenance programs.
Fuel taxes as well as other road user levies were periodically raised during the project
implementation period. A 1993 study estimated that total road user revenues grew from Rs
16.8 billion in 1989-90 to Rs 24.2 billion in 1992-93.
5.    The N-5 improvement contracts experienced significant completion delays and cost
overruns. The major causes included inadequate contractor prequalification, incomplete
engineering designs (e.g. median drainage works overlooked at bid stage, rehabilitation
contracts bid without detailed designs or construction drawings being available, etc.), lack of
timely completion of pre-construction activities (like land acquisition, utilities relocation etc.),
inadequate delegation of authority to the supervisory consultants, revision of highway
improvement priorities, inadequate initial pavement design, political interference and
unwillingness of the Employer to exercise contractual remedies against non-performing
contractors. The generic problem of inadequate counterpart funding continued to affect the
highway improvement and more particularly the maintenance program throughout the project.
Although the project did achieve significant annual increases in maintenance allocations from
the recurrent budget in the early years, aggregate allocations dropped from about 50% of the
network's maintenance needs to less than 33% by the end of the project. The pilot highway
patrol and driver instructor training school subcomponents for traffic safety could not be
implemented due to personality conflicts between Head of NTRC and senior Punjab Police
officials and the lack of certain high level policy decisions on part of the GOP during
implementation. However, funds for these activities were used to provide traffic safety
measures and remove black spots on sections of the N-5.
6.    One of the major institutional improvements achieved under the project related to
highway maintenance. Initiated with the assistance of expatriate technical assistance, the
highway maintenance organization has come into its own and is now recognized as an
important part of NHA. However, changes in its staffing and structure and somewhat
unplanned and premature decentralization of its operations to the regions in the early years
after reorganization affected its performance, which also remains susceptible to external factors
such as lack of maintenance funding. NHA management is currently reviewing the
effectiveness of its maintenance operations and putting in place improved institutional checks
and controls.
7.    The Bank's role during identification was quite effective, focusing on provision of the
policy framework necessary for development of the maintenance program, GOP's commitment
to fund it, mobilization of road user revenues, and maintaining a balance between rehabilitation
and new construction. However, the project covenants lacked specific linkage between
continued mobilization of road user charges and adequate funding of highway maintenance. A



4
comprehensive staff appraisal report served as a useful reference throughout implementation.
Bank supervision during implementation was intense and involved and played a key role in
successful completion of the project. Bank staff provided useful guidance to NHA on key
issues relating to procurement of construction contracts, pavement failures and road side
drainage, ensuring adequate budgetary resources, and even appointment of key staff. When
GOP launched major highway projects that had a questionable feasibility or which affected the
economic viability of ongoing project works, the Bank provided timely advice that helped GOP
in reviewing its decisions and prevented over commitment of its limited resources. The overall
performance of the Bank was satisfactory.
8.    The borrower's performance during identification and preparation were generally
satisfactory. During implementation, NHA's performance was mixed partly due to its
preoccupation with the major GOP highway expansion program initiated during 1991-93. The
role of the FHP Project Management Cell (PMC), assisted by expatriate consultants, was
crucial in keeping the project on track. NHA's performance on the maintenance
implementation program was handicapped by the limited financial allocations provided by the
GOP which remained short of the network's requirements. The planned transformation from
the "yardstick" based highway maintenance works previously carried out through the
provincial C&WD to the NHA managed contract maintenance was done smoothly. NHA staff
gradually took over management and operation of the highway maintenance program from the
expatriate consultants by the end of their assignment in 1993. NHA's performance was
satisfactory in terms of compliance with the project covenants.
9.    The principal objectives of the project were largely achieved. Improvements along the
N-5 highway lowered transportation costs and contributed towards increased traffic safety.
Institutional improvements were successfully implemented within NIA to plan and manage
highway maintenance and operations through the introduction of modern technical and
financial management practices. The construction industry received a powerful impetus as a
result of the project. The level of revenues mobilized from road users was periodically
increased, accompanied by a nominal growth of 14% per year in NHA maintenance
expenditures over the project period. The overall assessment of the project can be rated as
satisfactory.
Summary of Findings. Future Operations. and Key Lessons Learned
10.   The operation plan for all road sections and associated structures improved/constructed
under the project relies on existing institutional arrangement within NHA for maintenance
which are considered adequate subject to sufficient funding of the annual maintenance
program. After some initial delays, NHA has taken over the completed road sections for
maintenance.
11.   The following key lessons should be considered for future projects in the transport
sector in Pakistan:



5
a)    Project design should be kept simple, with manageable components that can be
handled through a single or a few agencies. Flexibility in implementation is desirable,
particularly for later years to allow for adjustments and improvements.
b)    Project preparation needs to be improved to minimize costly variations during
implementation. There is a need to develop cost effective pavement designs which are
appropriate for Pakistani conditions.
c)    Prequalification procedures need to be improved in line with the Bank's current
standard prequalification documents and rigorously implemented, to avoid awarding contracts
to firms lacking capacity. Contract adm.nistration should be streamlined and simplified.
Provision of dispute resolution boards should be included to minimize delays associated with
contractual disputes. Pre-construction activities for which the employer is responsible should
be completed before contract award.
d)    Highway sector investments should be carefully prioritized to ensure timely availability
of counterpart funds.
e)    Alternative off-budget highway maintenance arrangements are needed to provide an
adequate and reliable source of highway maintenance funding.
f)    Vehicle overloading must be effectively addressed to sustain highway network
improvements
g)    The use of local/in-country expertise should be maximized to reduce reliance on costly
long-term expatriate input over extended periods..

IMPLEMENTATION COMPLETION REPORT
PAKISTAN
FOURTH HIGHWAY PROJECT (LN.2814-PAK)
PART I - Project Implementation Assessment
Project Identity
Name:       FOURTH HIGHWAY PROJECT
Credit:     LN. 2814-PAK
RVP Unit:   South Asia
Country:    Pakistan
Sector:     Infrastructure
Sub-Sector:  Transportation
Background
1.    The improvement and modernization of key transport systems remain a critical factor in
the economic development of Pakistan. The country's agricultural, industrial and commercial
growth and export competitiveness are acutely affected by an inadequate and outmoded
infrastructure that increases transport cost by up to 50%. Although the railway can potentially
provide an economic and efficient means of transportation for long distance freight traffic, over
the last two decades it has lost a significant part of its modal share to roads - primarily due to
lack of institutional autonomy and commercially oriented management. Road transport has
emerged as the dominant mode of transport carrying about 85% of the inland passenger and
freight traffic.
2.    The National Highway Authority (previously National Highway Board) is responsible
for the 6,600 km long National Highways System. The 1,760 km N-5 highway along the
Karachi-Lahore-Peshawar corridor represents the main artery of the NHA network and carries
over 55% of the inter-city traffic in the country.
3.    This project was the fourth in a series of highway projects financed by the World Bank
since 1964. The First Highway Project (Cr. 54-PAK: US$ 17 million) involved construction of
142 km of highway between Karachi and Hyderabad. The project which was implemented
during 1964 - 68 introduced the design and construction of modern highways to Pakistan. The
Second Highway Project (Ln. 578-PAK: US$ 35 million) envisioning the construction of 270
km of highways was approved shortly thereafter, but was canceled in 1971 at GOP's request,
due to very high bids. It was not until the start of the Third Highway Project (Cr. 974-PAK:
US$ 50 million) in 1980, involving rehabilitation and construction of 296 km of highways, that
major highway construction was revived. The twelve year gap between the First and the Third
Highway Projects had a significant adverse impact on the road construction in Pakistan, as the



7
impetus given to the development of the local construction industry through the First Highway
Project was lost.
4.    The THP marked the beginning of sizable highway projects in the country. THP was
based on extensive feasibility studies and served as a stimulus for introducing modern
construction methods to local contractors. Problems associated with poor contract preparation
and management, and lack of experience in the use of modern construction equipment were
faced and overcome, and the first steps taken towards the adoption of modern highway
construction techniques.
Table A: Bank Assisted Highway Improvement Projects in Pakistan
PROJECT          IHP*              2HP              3HP               4HP
LOAN / CREDIT
AMOUNT           US$ 17 Million    US$ 35 Million   US$ 50 Million    US$ 152 Million

* IHP = First Highway Project
A. Statement/Evaluation of Objectives
5.    The principal objectives of the FBP were to: (a) lower transport costs on the Karachi -
Peshawar N-5 Highway by road rehabilitation; (b) develop the capacity of NHB (now NHA) to
plan and manage highway maintenance and operation, and to introduce modern technical and
financial management practices; (c) improve traffic safety on National Highways; (d) develop
the capacity of Pakistani contractors to execute maintenance and rehabilitation works using
modern techniques, equipment and materials; and (e) continue to mobilize revenues from road
users for the maintenance of the National Highways.
6.    The above objectives were to be pursued through: (i) rehabilitation of about 650 km of
the N-5 Highway and associated bridges; (ii) improvements to maintenance and traffic
engineering on National Highway; (iii) establishment of a pilot-scale highway patrol; (iv)
expansion of the national driver instructor training school; (v) institutional improvements in
Balochistan C&W Department; (vi) studies for future highway and road transportation
projects; and (vii) training and technical assistance.
7.    The project components were reasonably well linked to the project objectives; except
for component (v). Although not addressing any of the project objectives, this component was
apparently included to provide Balochistan some technical assistance, to partly offset the
adverse impact caused by NHB taking over maintenance of the highways under the project.
The Balochistan C&WD at the time had a large number of field staff responsible for force-
account maintenance of it highways who risked being rendered surplus by this move.



8
8.    Objectives (a), (b) and (e) were most relevant to the country's needs and consistent
with the Bank's strategy in the transport sector. Carrying over 55% of the country's inter-city
traffic, rehabilitation and capacity improvements in N-5 was bound to result in significant
savings in the vehicle operating costs and thereby lowering the transportation costs. Objective
(b) recognized the need to protect the country's major investments in the national highways
through establishment of a planned maintenance program, and the strengthening of the agency
to sustain this effort.
9.    Objective (c) was important as Pakistan suffered from a poor highway safety record,
and the proposed road improvements were expected to increase travel speeds and the
consequent accident risks, if proper traffic safety measures were not taken. Objective (d)
although desirable in itself was closely linked to the implementation of objective (a) and
continuation of the achievements under THP. Objective (e) was critical for the sustainability of
the physical assets created under the project.
B. Achievement of Obiectives
Lower Transport Cost on N-5
10.   One of the principal objectives of the project was lowering the vehicle operating cost
on N-5 through rehabilitation and improvements of 650 km of prioritized sections selected on
the basis of economic feasibility. This objective was substantially achieved as 504 km of N-5
was dualized or improved with lowering of the road roughness and resulting vehicle operating
costs.
Develop NHA Capacity - Modern Technical and Financial Management
11.   The project provided an excellent opportunity for NHA staft to work on a large
highway project. The technical, contractual and management challenges it offered helped staff
gain useful experience in highway construction and management. With constant assistance
from international project management consultants and under the guidance of Bank staff, NHA
personnel gradually learned to administer and supervise international contracts using FIDIC
conditions. The Project Management Cell (PMC) played a key role in ensuring the project's
continuity (particularly through periods of senior management changes within NHB/NHA),
with insuperable obstacles surmounted or side-stepped monthly. A comprehensive project
management system was established. Bidding and award procedures were streamlined and
contract documentation standardized. Detailed technical specifications and a composite
schedule of rates were developed. Procurement under Bank guidelines and ICB rules and
administering contracts being executed through seasoned expatriate contractors helped
improve construction management practices in the organization. The initial organizational
structure of the new National Highway Authority was also initially conceived and developed
under the FHP.



9
12.   Technical Numerous pavement related problems concerning: design; materials;
drainage; vehicle overloading; asphalt mix design; high ambient temperatures etc. were
encountered and successfully tackled, with assistance of international experts where necessary,
thereby providing a major learning experience for the NHA staff. As a result of the project,
NHA gained stature as a leading institution in the development and maintenance of highways in
Pakistan, and is now looked upon to provide guidance to provincial C&WDs and other road
development agencies of the country.
13.   Financial A Policies and Procedures manual, a Construction Administration manual
and an Accounting systems manual was developed by Consultants under the project which
included detailed financial management procedures. The policies and procedures manual was
updated in 1994 to address the need of NHA after reorganization. Internal audit controls were
established and technical assistance was provided to improve the accounting system. Although
the implementation of streamlined contract management procedures and financial management
reforms in NHA has been slow, trial adoption of the policies and procedures manual started in
February 1996, and the accounting assistance study financed under the follow-on TSP is
expected to introduce a computerized accounting system for the entire agency by FY96/97.
Develop NHA Capacity - Highway Maintenance
14.   A seven-year "Highway Maintenance Implementation Program" was carried out by
NHA during 1987-94 under the supervision of international maintenance consultants. The
program involved detailed assessment of the maintenance needs of the network through road
condition and pavement evaluation surveys to determine maintenance priorities. The
maintenance program was a structured and systematic Maintenance Intervention Level (MIL)
system based on the research carried out by the Transport Research Laboratory (TRL) of UK,
adjusted for local conditions. The MIL system and annual programs of "maintenance by
contract" were easily adopted by NHA and have worked successfully since 1990. The MIL
system was successfully used to identify priority sections for inclusion in the Resurfacing and
Strengthening Program (RSP) and Maintenance Backlog Reduction Program (MBRP) under
the follow-on Transport Sector Project (Ln.3241-PAK).
15.   The establishment of a Maintenance Directorate within NBB in late 1987 (which
changed to the Maintenance Wing after the creation of NHA in 1991) was the key to
institutionalizing maintenance in an organization that hitherto had focused only on the
development and expansion of the highway network. A Pavement Evaluation Unit (PEU) was
created to plan and execute annual road condition surveys under the MIL system. As
maintenance activities expanded following reorganization, a network of 16 field offices and
four regional offices were established manned by maintenance engineers responsible for
assessing pavement maintenance needs and carrying out contract maintenance works.
Responsibilities for maintenance management functions were gradually transferred from the
Consultants to NHA, by the end of the Consultants assignment in 1995.



10
Improve Traffic Safety
16.   Establishment of a traffic engineering unit in NHB in 1988, to complement the
maintenance management system marked an important step forward in terms of NHB's
capacity to address Traffic Safety issues. A manual on "Traffic Signs and Road Markings" was
developed and the highway improvement works were reviewed to provide junction
improvements, by-passes and some other traffic improvement measures in urban areas aimed at
relieving congestion and improving safety. The Highway Patrol and DITS subcomponents
could not be implemented. However, funds budgeted for these activities were used for
improving traffic safety and "black spot" removal on the N-5. The activities funded included
road signing, line marking, New Jersey type median barriers, fencing, junction improvements
and the channelization of commercial vehicles at major urban settlements.
17.   Another factor that helped in significantly improving traffic safety along the N-5 was
the separation of north and south bound traffic achieved as a result of dualization of over 350
km of highway under the project. This helped in reducing the incidence of fatal accidents -
particularly head-on collisions, which are a common occurrence on Pakistan's undivided
highways.
Develop Local Contractors' Capacity
18.   The FHP provided highly significant benefits for the local construction industry. While
the Pakistani construction industry generally gained from the FHP experience, two local
highway firms in particular emerged as major contractors. These firms have acquired large
fleets of equipment and skilled manpower, and are now competing internationally. However, it
was the smaller tiers of contractors that benefited most, through the opportunities created by
NHA's contract maintenance program. Routine maintenance works were carried out by local
district contractors, while the periodic maintenance contracts were awarded to suitably
qualified and equipped contractors. The contractors received on-the-job training in equipment
use and in various road maintenance functions through NHA's maintenance consultants. As
the maintenance program progressed, the number of contractors qualified to carry out these
works increased from about 250 in 1989 to nearly 1000 by end 1995. A number of contractors
have since graduated through the contract maintenance program and started competing for the
larger highway construction contracts. Some of the specialized contractors, particularly for
major bridge construction, also benefited by the opportunities provided under the project to
expand their operations and acquire modern equipment based technologies. As a result of the
project, NHA has developed a large roster of qualified Pakistani contractors in various tiers,
capable of undertaking significant sized highway maintenance and improvement programs.
Mobilize User Revenues for Maintenance
19.   Fuel taxes as well as other road user levies (MVT, registration and license fee and
tolls), were periodically raised during the project implementation period. The 1993 Haas



11
Study' estimated that total road user revenues grew from Rs 16.8 billion in 1989-90 to Rs 24.2
billion in 1992-93. The NHA Act (1991) empowered the authority to collect tolls on its
network, and tolls are now being collected on several bridges. However, the tolls which were
in effect before the Act on some highway sectors (e.g. Karachi-Hyderabad), continue to be
collected by the provincial governments.
20.   As a result of the increased institutional focus, and greater general awareness of the
importance of highway maintenance achieved through the project, NHB was able to obtain
increased annual maintenance allocations from MOF/GOP during the early years of the
project. Annual maintenance allocations to NHA increased by 210% (over 20% per year)
between 1987 and 1992). However, this increase could not be sustained during the subsequent
years and annual maintenance allocations dropped from about 50% to 33% of NHA's annual
demand by 1994-95. There was a nominal average growth of 14% per year in NHA
maintenance expenditures over the project period.
Economic Rate of Return (ERR)
21.   The revised ERR on completion of the project, using current vehicle operating costs
(VOC), value of time savings and NTRC's traffic count estimates, comes to 30.5% as
compared to 27% in the SAR. The difference is attributed to the higher benefits resulting from
a higher than anticipated growth in traffic, and increases in vehicle operating costs (as a result
of escalation in the prices of vehicles, spares and fuel). These have more than compensated for
the adverse impact of the increased project completion costs on the ERR.
D. Major Factors Affecting the Progress
N-5 Improvements Component
22.   Procurement Issues Inadequate contractor prequalification remained one of the
underlying causes of delay in completion of several contracts. The lack of objective evaluation
of the technical and financial capability of the contractors, particularly local contractors,
resulted for example in award of Contracts 7a and 7b to a local contractor without adequate
financial resources, despite adequate warning by the Bank. In the case of Contract 2, the
contract was awarded to the lowest bidder (an internationalUPakistani joint venture) although
the evaluation committee did not consider the joint venture qualified to perform. In both of
these cases significant delays and cost overruns were experienced before these contracts could
be completed. Inadequate sub-project preparation and design review prior to bid, led to
subsequent delays and claims on account of design changes and variation orders during
construction. For example, the overlay designs on the rehabilitation contracts (8b (I) and (ii))
encountered pavement failures and had to undergo substantial revisions following award,
which resulted in significant completion delays and major cost increases. Inadequate advance
planning by NHA was another factor affecting the contracts. A number of pre-construction
activities like land acquisition, relocation of utilities, removal of encroachments from the right
' Study of the Arterial Highway Network of Pakistan carried out by M/s Haas in 1993



12
of way, cutting of road side trees (requiring high level GOP approval) etc. were often carried
out after contract award, resulting in significant delays.
23.   Inadequate Counterpart Funding Although a detailed exercise was carried out at the
initial stages of the project to establish counterpart funding needs taking into account the GOP
capacity to meet their obligations, and agreements were reached with the Ministry of Finance
in this regard, timely availability of counterpart funding continued to be a problem throughout
the life of the project. One of the reasons was an ambitious highway development program
launched by the GOP during 1991-93 which included, besides other major projects, a six-lane
340 km long Motorway from Lahore to Islamabad. In certain instances, the lack of GOP funds
led to delay of award2 and eventually resulted in higher project completion costs. From
another perspective, the delays in award of major pavement rehabilitation contracts (8b(i) and
8b(ii)) resulted in further deterioration and led to complete reconstruction at an additional cost
nearly 50% over the average cost of round 2 contracts. Significant delays were also
encountered in payments to FHP contractors and consultants during the final years of the
project, on account of inadequate Rupee funding available with NHA to implement a very
large portfolio of ongoing contracts.
24.   Cumbersome Contract Administration Although the FHP contracts were based on
FIDIC conditions, significant changes were incorporated in the special conditions limiting the
authority of 'the Engineer'. All decisions related to approval of additional costs, issuance of
variation orders, certifying payments and issuing substantial completion, defects liability
certificate were retained by the executing agency. This resulted in undue delays in approval
and completion of works. The delays in issuance of defects liability certificates, which in some
cases has exceeded eighteen months, hindered the take over of these works by the NHA
Maintenance Wing.
25.   Revised Priorities A total of eight major contracts (No 1, 2, 3A, 6A, 6B, 7A, 7B and
8A), for construction of 355 km additional carriageway and four major bridges, were awarded
by 1989 in two rounds of bidding involving Bank's ICB procedures. In 1990 contracts 3B, 4
and 5 were dropped from the project and replaced by rehabilitation contracts 8B-I and -II
between Gujranwala and Rawalpindi. This was done as traffic on the N-5 sections north of
Hala did not grow at the projected rate, while rapid deterioration was experienced on N-5
between Gujranwala and Rawalpindi due to high and rapidly growing traffic volumes and axle
loads.
26.   Asphalt Pavement Design The progress was adversely affected by the problems related
to pavement design. Asphalt Concrete represented a relatively new technology in Pakistan.
There was little experience in the use of material under high temperatures and excessively
heavy axle loads with very high tire pressures. The original FHP pavement designs were
largely based on 1972 AASHTO Guidelines for flexible pavements. Major pavement related
failures were encountered on a few contracts shortly after opening to traffic, necessitating
review of these designs by an international pavement expert. NHA was advised to supplement
2Contracts 8b(i) and (ii) originally part of round 2 (Jan 89) were postponed and included in round 3 (Oct 91).



13
current design approaches with empirical-analytical design using mechanistic methods. This
has led to a significant change in the pavement designs adopted by NHA for all new projects,
and resulted in the use of thicker asphalt layers and higher unit costs.
27.   Highway Drainage Prior to dualizing N-5, the single carriageway was cambered to
drain on either side of the road. However, with the construction of a second carriageway,
median drainage became an important design consideration. This aspect of drainage was
unfortunately overlooked in the original FHP contract designs, and the resulting inadequate
drainage at times contributed to pavement failures. Bank staff were instrumental in
highlighting this problem, bringing it to the notice of NHA and its consultants during
supervision missions, and asking for the inclusion of missing drainage works in the contracts,
through variation orders if necessary. Another problem related to the lack of adequate
'daylighting' of aggregate base and subbase layers. This led to pavement distress on certain
contracts, particularly after heavy rains, necessitating expensive repairs and delaying project
completion. Bank staff have advised NHA to standardize use of "day-lighted" base and sub-
base layers in all future contracts.
28.   Unwillingness to Apply Contractual Remedies The prevailing contractual environment
in Pakistan, makes it extremely difficult for an Employer to exercise his contractual authority
to rescind a contract in the event of contractor's non-performance, or to apply remedies clearly
stated in the contract. The experience on contract 7a clearly demonstrates how easy it is for an
affected contractor to seek a court injunction against such termination, and force the Employer
to seek an out-of court settlement or risk major completion delays.
The various factors detailed above resulted in significant completion delays on most of the
FHP contracts. These delays, which ranged from 5% to 165% of the original contract
completion period, forced NHA to seek a one year extension of the loan closing date, to
complete the project.
Highway Maintenance Component
29.   Vehicle Overloading A continuing modal shift in freight traffic from road to rail, as
well as a trend towards increased vehicle overloading during the project period, caused
accelerated pavement deterioration and resulted in a significant annual increase in maintenance
funding requirements.
30.   GOP Funding The main problem affecting implementation of this vital component
was lack of adequate annual funding for routine and periodic maintenance throughout the
project period. The following table shows a comparison of the maintenance funding
requirements versus the actual allocation/expenditures:



14
Table B: Requirement for Maintenance Funds and Expenditures
(in Pak Rs)
Year             Requirement Based on Network               Expenditures
Needs
1987-88                     347,000                            180,000
1988-89                     422,770                            200,680
1989-90                     474,500                            268,850
1990-91                     600,000                            281,595
1991-92                     795,500                            378,000
1992-93                     930,000                            410,000
1993-94                    1,097,000                           430,500
1994-95                    1,317,000                           452,000
Maintenance expenditures increased at an average annual rate of around 20% in the period
between 1987-92, before tapering off during the later part of the project period. Although
annual routine and periodic maintenance allocations to NHA from the GOP recurrent budget
remained substantially short of requirements, the gap was largely made up through a series
capital maintenance programs (MBRP/RSP, 1992 Flood Damage Restoration etc.) with the
result that the overall condition of the NHA network (as indicated by the MIL survey)
remained fairly stable between 1989 and 1994.
Table C: Overall Condition of the National Highway Network
(as a percentage of total network length)
Maintenance Type                  1989              1991             1994
Network % requiring Routine Maintenance        48               34                51
Network % requiring Periodic Maintenance       31               44                30
Network % requiring Reconstruction             21               22                19
(Note: Total network length surveyed in 1994 was 6196 Km.)
31.   NHA Reorganization The reorganization of NHB into NHA in 1991, although a vital
step for the agency, was carried out at a time that was most inappropriate for the newly
created Maintenance Directorate, which was still in its early stages of development and had
just started being recognized as an important part of NHB. Frequent changes in the role and
structure of the maintenance organization affected its performance and staff morale. The
problem was compounded by the somewhat haphazard and premature decentralization of
maintenance operations to the regions. The degree of attention needed during these early days,
for timely resolution of issues and to institutionalize the new concepts, could have been
managed far better from the NHA headquarters in Islamabad, close to the NHA decision
makers and the maintenance consultants. This resulted in significant delays in implementation
of the maintenance program, lowered the quality of maintenance and led to an erosion of some
of the benefits realized during the earlier period. NHA management is currently reviewing the
effectiveness of its maintenance operations and intends to put in place improved checks and
controls to address some of the quality control issues that have resulted from decentralization
of maintenance to the regions.
32.   Expatriate Technical Assistance The project benefited from very substantial expatriate
specialist inputs for implementation of both the rehabilitation and maintenance component,



15
particularly during the early years. However, as the implementation progressed, the transition
to the use of locally available professional expertise was rather slow, with the result that the
original Technical Assistance allocations were exceeded.
Traffic Safety Component
33.   The proposed pilot-scale Highway Patrol aimed at controlling moving violations and
improving traffic safety could not take off as NTRC (the coordinating agency) was unable to
reach a satisfactory implementation agreement with the Punjab Police. The problem stemmed
from the jurisdictional conflict between the federal Ministry of Communications and the
provincial Home Department which managed the police. But the primary problem was
personality conflicts between Head of NTRC and senior Punjab police officials and the lack of
a high level policy decision by GOP which could have helped resolve the inter-agency
relationships and operating procedures.
34.   The Driver Instructor Training School (DITS) was dropped from the loan during the
implementation phase, because of lack of a long-term plan integrating the DITS into the Road
Safety strategy. Main concerns related to the sustainability of such an institution in terms of
the recurrent cost of the facility; the number of instructors trained yearly and their demand in
the private sector driving schools; and GOP policy regarding mandating recruitment of
certified instructors in these driver training schools.
35.   Assistance to Balochistan C&WD This component involved technical assistance to the
Government of Balochistan C&WD to improve construction and maintenance through quality
control and testing. Two expatriate staff members carried out this work with little support
from C&WD. A set of manuals were prepared for field and laboratory testing and pavement
design. In addition, a comprehensive report was prepared on use of the extensive equipment
resources available with the C&WD. This component ended abruptly with the demise of one
of the specialists, who was not replaced. C&WD failed to benefit from the technical assistance
C. Project Sustainability
36.   Institutional Reforms The Fourth Highway Project in a few short years saw the
transformation of the NHB from an agency with a small staff and a limited agenda for
development of the major national highways, to an autonomous highway authority responsible
for planning and implementing Pakistan's multi-billion dollar highway development program,
and reporting directly to the National Highway Council headed by the Prime Minister. The
NHA is already playing an increasingly important role in developing highway sector policies
including international transit agreements with neighboring countries, and is beginning to focus
attention on resource mobilization and the involvement of private sector. The reorganization
of NHA along functional lines has been approved by the National Highway Council, trial
adoption of the Policies and Procedures manual is underway, internal audit controls have been
instituted, and accounting procedures are being improved. A private sector cell has been
created to encourage private sector investment in highway development and operations. The



16
NHA in conjunction with the MOC has developed draft legislation for traffic safety and axle-
load controls on highways. The foregoing amply indicates that the institutional improvements
initiated under the project have borne fruit and helped NHA emerge as a viable organization
with a global view and greater commitment to the sustainable development of the highway
sector.
37.   The establishment of a separate road maintenance wing and traffic engineering
functions within NHA are among the features of the project that will help ensure sustainability
of the physical improvements achieved under the project. The introduction and widespread
acceptance of a maintenance management system featuring annual MIL surveys and further
improvements that are currently underway within NHA towards the development of a
comprehensive pavement management system (PMS) are strong indicators of the sustainability
of the project initiated maintenance implementation program.
38.   Physical Outputs The physical works completed under the project are being handed
over to the maintenance wing for inclusion under the annual maintenance program. However,
the current institutional arrangements for financing maintenance which rely exclusively on
budgetary transfers from GOP are unreliable. The Bank is now working with NHA on the
preparation of a proposed Highway Rehabilitation Project that aims to put in place new
institutional arrangements to ensure adequate financing of priority road maintenance
expenditures, independent of the budget.
D. Bank Performance
39.   The Bank played an effective role in project identification. Bank missions focused on:
the policy framework required for development of a maintenance program; GOP's
commitment to fund its implementation; mobilization of road user revenues to fund
rehabilitation and maintenance; and establishing a balance between rehabilitation and new
construction under the project. The Bank's strategy in the highway sub-sector, which
emphasized sustainable development, provided the basic framework for project design. During
project preparation, Bank staff helped ensure a consistent approach towards the feasibility
studies, particularly economic evaluation, that were carried out by several local consultants
under the overall supervision of an international consultant..
40.   The project objectives were appropriate and clear, and staff assistance to the borrower
during project preparation was generally satisfactory. However, project covenants did not
provide an explicit linkage between the continued mobilization of road user charges, and
adequate funding for highway maintenance. The proposed institutional improvements in
C&WD Balochistan appear to have been an effort to include some regional balance, but this
component had no bearing on any of the other project objectives. The Bank produced a
comprehensive staff appraisal report that addressed various aspects of the project and served
as a useful reference throughout implementation.



17
41.   Through fifteen supervisory missions carefully spaced during implementation, the
Bank's supervision was intense and involved, and played a major role in successful completion
of the project. These missions, though carrying a larger agenda involving other projects in the
transport/highway sector, helped in keeping the objectives in focus during implementation.
From the initial stages of the project, Bank staff kept a keen eye on procurement, and provided
guidance to NHA on key issues relating to the award of construction contracts. NHA was
encouraged to take timely decisions on contractors' claims regarding cost escalation and re-
rating that were critical in completion of the major contracts within the project period. At
times, Bank missions even went on record in urging NHB/NHA to appoint eligible and
deserving staff with a proven record of performance to key positions to ensure sustainability of
the new initiatives, particularly the maintenance program. On technical matters, the Bank's
decision to fund an international expert to study the pavement failures, and useful advice given
by Bank staff during supervision missions, particularly regarding drainage issues, helped
immensely in improving the quality of the completed works in particular, and highway design
practices in general.
42.   Learning from past experience, the Bank remained cognizant of the GOP's limited
ability to provide counterpart funding throughout the project, and took timely steps in this
regard. It advised the GOP to hold fuel prices in case of lowering of international oil prices
which helped in ensuring adequate budgetary resources for counterpart funding. When the
GOP decided to launch a major construction program for the N-55 Indus Highway (that runs
parallel to N-5 on the west bank of river Indus), the Bank advised against this undertaking,
highlighting that GOP risked over-committing its resources and diverting N-5 traffic to the
new highway, thereby lowering the economic viability of the project. During a resource
crunch in the country as a result of the Gulf war, the Bank showed flexibility by allowing an
accelerated rate of disbursement that financed 70% of the cost of civil works, instead of the
original 51%, which helped ensure uninterrupted implementation of the project. During round
two of bidding, the Bank advised NHB to postpone the award of one of the contracts and
return unopened bids, as the available counterpart funding was not adequate. During 1991-92
when GOP launched a major highway expansion program, the Bank advised against the
construction of multi-billion dollars multi-lane motorways in the absence of significant traffic
volumes. This contributed to the eventual government's decision to drop the Islamabad -
Peshawar motorway project. In summary, the overall performance of the Bank was
satisfactory.
E. Borrower Performance
43.   The project was identified and prepared jointly by NHB and the Bank, building on the
achievements and experiences of the Third Highway Project and keeping in view the country's
development needs in the highway subsector. NHB's performance during identification was
satisfactory. However, during project preparation, incomplete engineering designs of some
parts of the project's physical works (e.g. lack of median drainage in bid drawings, Round 3
pavement rehabilitation contracts bid without detailed drawings, etc. ) led to subsequent
implementation delays and increases in completion cost.



18
44.   During implementation NHB/NHA's performance was mixed, as it practically grew
during the eight-year life of the project from a small agency with limited staff resources and a
modest development portfolio, to a major actor in Pakistan's transport sector with a large and
growing portfolio of projects. After a good start, the desired level of attention needed from
NHA senior management for expeditious project implementation could not be maintained
during 1991-93, as NHA embarked on a major highway expansion program that included the
Lahore - Islamabad motorway and the Lahore Bypass projects. Added to this was the
reorganization of the agency along functional lines which, although a positive step, affected the
performance of NHA during the transition phase. However, the role of the Project
Management Cell (PMC), was crucial in keeping project implementation on track, particularly
during this difficult period.
45.   Most of the major civil work contracts for construction/rehabilitation of the N-5 faced
completion delays and cost overruns due to poor contractor prequalification, inadequate pre-
construction preparation, and at times weak contract administration. However, the project
works were largely completed within the extended project period, the loan was fully disbursed,
the quality of the constructed works was generally quite good, and the project helped establish
improved construction practices within the construction industry.
46.   On the maintenance implementation program NHB/NHA's performance was
constrained primarily due to lack of adequate annual budgetary allocations for maintenance, a
factor largely beyond NHA's control. The switch over from the "yardstick" based maintenance
work carried out under force-account arrangements by the provincial C&WDs to a
"measurement based" contract maintenance program was carried out quite smoothly. This was
partly due to the fact that NHB had no departmental labor force, equipment or facilities to
dispose off, but also due to the efforts that went in planning the transition. NHB/NHA's
success in institutionalizing maintenance in its organization, adoption of the MIL system,
implementation and expansion of the contract maintenance program to its entire network, and
more recent efforts aimed at developing a comprehensive pavement management system, have
been commendable.
47.   Starting with FY 1993-94, NHA introduced on a trial basis, lump sum "Term and
Length" typ contracts for routine maintenance. These contracts were awarded through
competitive bidding for specific segments of the highway for a fixed period of one year. No
quantities were included in the contract, instead a list of work items formed the basis of the
contract. Monthly payments were based on inspections. Results to date have been mixed.
NHA management is currently reviewing the comparative advantages of the Term and Length
approach versus the conventional measured contracts to determine which course of action to
adopt for the future.
48.   Under the Highway Maintenance Implementation Program, NHA officials worked
closely with the Maintenance Consultants and gradually took over their local PEU engineering
staff and management and operation of the highway maintenance program, by the end of the
Consultants assignment in 1993. As the requirement for improvement of the existing highway



19
network to cater for growing traffic and heavy axle loads increased, NHA felt the need to
develop the MIL system into a comprehensive Pavement Management System (PMS). PEU
staff has now been transferred to a newly established Pavement Management Cell which is
working towards this objective. The expanded PMS is being used to identify highway sections
for inclusion in the proposed Bank assisted Highway Rehabilitation Project.
49.   NHA's performance in terms of complying with the project covenants was largely
satisfactory. All covenants were complied within the deadline. Overall, NHA's performance
during implementation was satisfactory.
50.   The consultants (for project management, maintenance management and contract
supervision) made a significant contribution in assisting NHA achieve the principle objectives
of the project. It was this joint partnership between NHA and the consultants that led to the
successful conclusion of the project. The overall performance of the consultants was
satisfactory.
51.   Except for the contractors working on Contract 7a and Contract 2, the performance of
the construction contractors was largely satisfactory - given the originally weak state of the
domestic construction industry - even though completion of several of the FHP contracts was
delayed beyond the original date. The performance of the joint venture who were awarded
Contract 2 was affected by the split of the joint-venture first and by serious cash-flow problems
of the remaining partner later; in addition, some sections of the road showed severe pavement
failures shortly after opening and had to be reconstructed by a local contractor.
F. Assessment of Outcome
52.   The principal objectives of the project were largely achieved. Besides significantly
reducing the transportation cost on the N-5 by road rehabilitation and construction, the
improvements along the N-5 highway also contributed significantly in improving the traffic
safety through increased capacity; and the separation of the north and south bound traffic.
Institutional improvements within NHA to plan and manage highway maintenance and
operation through the introduction of modern technical and financial management practices
were successfully achieved. NHA's reorganization along functional lines combined with
adoption of policies and procedural reforms will go a long way in enabling NHA to implement
the country's development objectives in the highway subsector. The highway maintenance
program has been institutionalized over the past several years, but NHA still requires and
adequate and stable source of highway maintenance funding. The major achievement of the
maintenance program was not only generating awareness of the importance of proper
maintenance among all those involved in the development of the road sector in the country, but
also showing the way how it can be done in a cost effective manner in the face of innumerable
constraints. This emphasis on highway maintenance generated by the project helped mobilize
some additional share of revenues collected from road users for highway maintenance in the
early years of project implementation.



20
53.   The construction industry received a powerful impetus as a result of the project. A
large number of maintenance contractors have emerged. Several have grown and are now
equipped to carry out large works, enhancing the domestic construction industry's capacity to
implement future rehabilitation and maintenance programs.
54.   The project was completed within one year of the original target and the entire loan
was disbursed. Despite construction delays and substantial increase in the scope of work on
some contracts, the highway improvement works remained quite cost effective, and compared
favorably in terms of unit cost with other similar contracts awarded by GOP during the project
period. Table D provides the cost per kilometer of completed additional carriageway and
major rehabilitation works let out under three rounds of bidding.
Table D: Completed Projects - Cost per Kilometer
Bid           Contract      Awarded       Avg. Cost Per Km      Remarks
Round         No            (Mo/Yr)       (Mill Rs)
1             1, 3a, 7a, 7b    Nov-Dec/87    8.02       Additional carriageway
2             2, 6a, 6b     Jan-Jun/89    9.76           Additional carriageway
3             8b(i), 8b(ii)    Oct/91     13.593         Reconstruction/ rehabilitation
55.    On balance, the overall assessment of the project can be rated as quite satisfactory.
G. Future Operations
56.   NHA has developed an Operation Plan for the assets created under the project, which
primarily consists of all road sections and associated structures improved/constructed under
various contracts (A copy of the plan is enclosed in Appendix A). The plan does not require
any additional staffing as the existing institutional arrangements for maintenance are considered
adequate. However, additional maintenance funding provision over the next five years has
been indicated in line with the maintenance needs of other roads of similar age. The actual
maintenance need will be assessed annually as part of NHA's annual maintenance/pavement
condition surveys.
57.   The contractual maintenance periods for most of the work completed under the project
have now elapsed and Defect Liability Certificates have been issued, after some initial delay.
NHA maintenance section has finally taken over these sections for routine maintenance starting
from FY 1996-97. Some sections of the road which have now been open to traffic for the last
3The cost under these contracts on the Gujranwala - Rawalpindi section of N-5 was high due to: major changes
in pavement design; extra thickness of asphalt to provide desired cross slope for drainage; increase in
reconstruction and major rehabilitation instead of overlay; and inclusion of high priority drainage and traffic
safety works.



21
couple of years (since substantial completion) without routine maintenance contracts being in
effect, have begun to show the signs of this neglect; and will require careful attention during
FY 1996-97 to make good the past lapse.
58.   The monitoring indictors identified for the operation plan are adequate to evaluate the
performance of the plan, and are sustainable as they rely on the information routinely collected
by NHA and NTRC. NHA has been requested to have the information on indicators compiled
periodically, and shared with the Bank.
H. Key Lessons Learned
59.   The experience with implementation of the highway safety component, highlights the
need to keep project designs simple. Components should be limited to those that can be
implemented through a single or a few agencies. Also, project design should be flexible, to
allow for adjustments and improvements during implementation. It is not prudent to include
detailed and contract specific implementation plans for works expected to be undertaken in the
outer years at the time of initial project preparation.
60.   Use improved pre-qualification procedures, as outlined in the Bank's current standard
prequalification documents. Avoid awarding contracts to firms that lack adequate capacity.
During the bid evaluation process, ensure reasonable rates before recommending a contract
for award. Ensure that pre-construction activities for which the Employer is responsible (e.g.
land acquisition, relocation of utilities, etc.), are completed before contract award.
61.   Improve project preparation and detailed designs to minimize variations during
implementation. Develop cost effective pavement options for Pakistani conditions. For heavily
trafficked roads in particular, asphalt concrete pavement designs using the 1986 AASHTO
Design Guide may need to be cross-checked using mechanistic procedures, due to excessive
axle loads and very high tire pressures and climatic considerations. Ensure drainage is fully
addressed during the design process.
62.   Carefully prioritize highway sector investments to ensure availability of adequate
counterpart funds for timely implementation.
63.   Adopt simplified and streamlined construction administration procedures. Include the
provision of dispute resolution boards in contracts to minimize the need for either party to seek
legal recourse from courts and the risk of related delays.
64.   There is a need to put in place reliable off-budget arrangements for adequately funding
highway maintenance. Inadequate funding for road maintenance remained a constant hurdle for
NHA throughout the project period, resulting in less than complete implementation of
meticulously prepared maintenance programs.



22
65.   To ensure the integrity of the highway network, the issue of vehicle overloading and
axle load control must also be addressed. The July 1995 NTRC axle load study confirms an
alarming rise in vehicle overloading and excessive axle loads resulting in accelerated
deterioration of the pavements. The GOP is considering new legislation (Highway Safety Act)
to give it legal authority to check and control vehicle overloading. However, progress has
been slow. Enforcement of axle load control will require several supporting measures such as:
(i) a network of weigh-stations to monitor vehicle overloading; (ii) fiscal disincentives to
discourage use of offending vehicles; (iii) a revised tariff structure that makes road user
charges proportional to pavement damage; coupled with (iv) self-regulation by the trucking
industry.
66.   The use of local/in-country expertise should be maximized to reduce reliance on long-
term expatriate inputs. Pakistani consulting firms have benefited significantly from FHP, in
terms of design skills and contract supervision experience. In many areas their engineering
skills can now be compared with consulting firms from the developed countries. Expatriate
input in future projects should be limited to specialist skills that are not available locally.
I. Evaluation of Program Obiectives Categories
67.   Poverty reduction, gender issues, other social objectives and environmental objectives
were not directly related to the project and hence were not evaluated.

Appendix A
IMPLEMENTATION COMPLETION REPORT
PAKISTAN
FOURTH HIGHWAY PROJECT
(Ln. 2814-PAK)
Mission's Aide-Memoire (with Operation Plan)



PAKISTAN
FOURTH HIGHWAY PROJECT - (LOAN 2814-PAK)
and
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT - (LOAN 3241-PAK)
(Highway Component Only)
Supervision Mission, October 1995
Aide Memoire
(FHP No. 15 & TSP No. 11)
1.    During the period October 15, through November 2, 1995, a World Bank
mission comprising Messrs. Navaid A. Qureshi (Mission Leader), Hasan Masood
and B. P. Kennedy reviewed progress on the Fourth Highway Project (FHP) and
Transport Sector Project (TSP) (highway component only) and discussed and
agreed a schedule for the preparation of an Implementation Completion Report
(ICR) for the Fourth Highway Project. Messrs. Hardyal Singh and Amanullah K.
Malik joined the mission part-time for some meetings. The mission also held
preliminary discussions with GOP/NHA regarding a proposed Highway
Rehabilitation Project and agreed on the preparatory steps needed, and on a
timetable for their completion. The Aide Memoire records the findings of the
mission and records the agreements and understandings reached among the
officials of the National Highway Authority (NHA) and the mission. It is
subject to confirmation by Bank management.
2.    The World Bank Mission members wish to record their appreciation for the
courtesies and cooperation extended to them by the officials of the NHA in
headquarters and in the Provinces and for the excellent field trip
arrangements (the field trip itinerary appears as Attachment 1).
3.    This combined Aide-Memoire is arranged as follows:
I.           Fourth Highway Project (FHP)
II.          Transport Sector Project (TSP)
III          Proposed Highway Rehabilitation Project
Attachments



2
I. FOURTE HIGEWAY PROJECT (Ln.2814-PAK)
(Final Aide Memoire No.15)
1.    The project will be substantially completed by December 31, 1995, the
extended Closing Date for Loan 2814-PAK. Minor works contracts related to
shoulder protection, fencing and drainage and some ancillary works on
Contracts 7a, and 8B(i) amounting to about Rs 200 m (3% of total project cost)
will be completed after this date with GOP financing. The project is generally
in compliance with the legal covenants. The actions agreed during the December
1994 Supervision mission have largely been met, with the exception of the
action relating to the provision of additional maintenance funding for
FY95/96. Attachment 2 is an extract from the October, 1995 Project Management
Cell (PMC) Progress Report indicating the overall status of the sub-projects.
There is a brief discussion below of the ongoing project components.
Project Costs
2.    The current overall project financial position is summarized below:

Expenditure and Counterpart Funding.
3.    Attachment 2 also gives the status of Disbursements, details of
completion costs and required GOP and Loan financing for project completion as
of Sep. 30, 1995. Expenditures of about Rs 190 m (Rs 130 m GOP) were incurred
on FHP during the first quarter of FY9S/96. The mission estimates that about
Rs 640 m. (incl. Rs. 490 m as GOP share)2 would be required beyond September
1995 to complete the project. Of this amount, civil works and consultants
expenditures of.approximately Rs_465 m are expected to be incurred by Dec. 31,
1995. The balance work (of about Rs 175 m) will have to be fully financed by
NRA from GOP sources. Since an amount of only Rs 100 m was allocated to FHP in
NRA's 95/96 PSDP, additional allocations will be required. NHA has already
requested GOP to provide the necessary funds to expeditiously complete these
works. NRA management and the mission agreed that ti) all efforts will be made
1 Incldiaing Rs 146 m in NHA establishment costs.
