n. '7



3
to fully utilize the remaining loan amount by the closing date; and (ii) the
scope of the remaining works would be reviewed immediately and reduced to the
absolute minimum by eliminating non-essential items.
Achievement of Project Objectives
4.    The project objectives were to:
a) lower vehicle operating costs on the N-5 highway;
b) strengthen the NHB, now the NHA;
c) improve road safety;
d) develop the domestic contractors; and
e) mobilize revenues for maintaining national roads.
The ICR will provide a comprehensive evaluation of the project. However, some
highlights of the project's impact are now evident.
5.    Objectives (a), (c) and (d) have been substantially achieved and the
significant progress made on objectives (b) and (e) is continuing. A total
length of about 343 Km of the most important national road, the N-5 has been
dualized and an additional 107 Km of the existing carriageway has been
rehabilitated, strengthened and resurfaced. In addition, a total of 23 major
bridges on this road have been completed under the project. These improvements
to the N-5 road have resulted in major benefits to the economy of Pakistan
through savings in vehicle operating costs on the new and improved roads and
bridges.
6.    Furthermore, the additional capacity provided on the N-5 and the
rehabilitation and bridge works, have resulted in a notable improvement in
road safety. The road and bridge works have been designed and built to higher
and safer standards and have relieved congestion and reduced accidents by
separating the north/south traffic. A further contribution to road safety has
been made by the construction of junction improvements, by-passes and some
lay-bys at urban areas to relieve congestion.
7.    These economic benefits have resulted from very cost-effective
investments in the highway subsector. The completion costs of the additional
carriageway contracts range from Rs 5 m to Rs 13 m per Km. The estimated costs
of the rehabilitation, reconstruction and overlay works on Contracts 8b(i) and
8b(ii) average around Rs 12 m per Km.
8.    The development of a strong domestic contracting industry has received a
powerful impetus under the project. Several local contractors have developed
into strong well resourced major contractors who can now compete against
international contractors. Some are now beginning to expand their operations
outside Pakistan. Smaller contractors working on routine and periodic
maintenance contracts have received training and assistance under the project.
Some have now.acquired-equipment A(including hot-mix asphalt p'tants) and are
operating at a-higher_level. In addition, the expertise and experience of
local consultants have been enhanced through their involvement in the design
and supervision of-the project components.
9.    Progress has been -made in strengthening the NRA, which replaced the NHB
*            in'1991.' Policies and Procedures and Construction Administration Manuals
r            prepared under the FHP to enable the new Authority to efficiently manage the
national road system are now in the process of being adopted. Staff positions,



4
previously only on a temporary basis, have now been regularized and staff
morale has much improved. Assistance for the institutional strengthening of
NHA is continuing under the Transport Sector Project.
10.   Another principal objective of the project has been achieved in regard
to NHA's capability for highway maintenance. The Pavement Evaluation Unit
(PEU) set up under the project has established a Maintenance Intervention
Level (MIL) scoring system which indicates annually the routine, periodic
maintenance and rehabilitation needs of the national road network and the
related funding requirements. The MIL survey has been done annually for the
past 5 years and allows NHA to monitor the condition of its network. NRA is
now in the process of expanding the MIL system into a more comprehensive
Pavement Management System (PMS). FHP has also introduced the system of
"maintenance-by-contract" within NRA, which has resulted in major efficiencies
over the previously used force account system. Furthermore, NHA has developed
"term and length" Routine Maintenance contracts over the last two years.
Unfortunately, the advantages of the MIL maintenance management system and the
more efficient maintenance by contract approach have not been fully recognized
within GOP, as evidenced by the increasing gap between NRA's annual
maintenance funding requirements and allocations for road maintenance.
Civil Works
11.   All the major civil works contracts have been completed except for
Contracts 7A, 8b(i) and 8b(ii). Substantial completion of these contracts is
expected by end December 1995. Minor contracts for essential median drainage,
fencing and treatment of shoulders were started in early 1995. Implementation
has been slow, with the result that about 65% of these works are now expected
extend beyond the closing date. Attachment 2 includes the mission's estimate
of balance work beyond December 31, 1995.
Contract 7a
12.   The contract is about 84% complete. Progress remains painfully slow. The
latest extension to the contract period expired on December 31, 1994. The main
pavement works have been completed and only a small quantity of aggregate base
and asphaltic paving work at Gujranwala bypass and bituminous treatment of
shoulders remains. The contractor apparently has serious cash flow problems.
NRA is considering contractual options for completing the remaining work.
Contracts 8b(i) and 8b(ii)
13.   Contract 8b(i) for the rehabilitation and resurfacing of about 88 km of
the N-5 between Gujranwala and Jhelum and some 2.5 km at Shahdara, is expected
to be substantially completed by end December 1995.
14.   Contract 8b(ii) covering about 61 km of the N-5 between Jhelum and
Rawalpindi, was substantially completed in February 1995. Some pavement
settlements-have occurred following the recent heavy rains. The lack of
effective median drainage appears to have contributed to the problem. NRA was
of the view that settlements have primarily occurred due to the incorporation
of the existing high embankments into the new road. NHA has investigated the
matter and is expected to direct commencement of remedial works shortly.
x.",% * .   -  .    ..



5
Drainage Problems
15.   The mission again strongly recommends that NRA introduce median drainage
for dual carriageways and full day-lighting of the pavement structures as a
standard design feature in order to prevent the recurrence of costly failures.
Maintenance
16.   The mission asked NRA to implement the standard maintenance strategy at
the field DD level (as far as possible), to ensure better consistency when
preparing initial annual maintenance estimates.
17.   During 1995/96 GOP allocated only Rs 475 m to NRA for maintenance,
against the agency's request for Rs 1554 m. The allocation is barely 5% above
last year's figure and represents a reduction in real terms. Inadequate
maintenance allocations by GOP continue to remain a major issue in the highway
subsector. This issue will be addressed directly in the proposed Highway
Rehabilitation Project.
Studies.
Axle Load Study.
18.   The axle load survey was conducted by the National Transport Research
Centre (NTRC) at 30 stations on the National Highway network between March and
September 1994 and the final report was issued in July 1995. The findings of
the survey are alarming. The survey finds that there has been a significant
increase in vehicle axle loads since 1982. The proportion of commercial
(         vehicles to total traffic ranges from 6% to 76%, with an average of 35%. Two
axle and three axle vehicles now account for 69% and 21% of the total
commercial traffic. This compares with a share of 96% and 1% respectively in
1982, and confirms the significant increase in the use of multi-axle vehicles
observed in recent years. About 88% of the rear axle loads surveyed exceed 8
tons, 74% exceed 10 tons and 43% exceed 12 tons. The average damaging effect
on pavements as measured by the equivalent standard axle (ESA) factor for a
two-axle truck has increased by almost 40% (up from 3.37 in 1982 to 4.67 in
1994). Since the damaging effect of axles exceeding 8 tonnes increases by the
power of four, the consequences in terms of the costs of pavement construction
and maintenance are disturbing. Following completion of this survey, NRA are
currently identifying the a) costs to the economy of this continued
overloading and b) the optimum axle load appropriate to conditions in
Pakistan. The Terms of Reference for the optimum axle load identification
study would be agreed with the Bank, and this work will be completed by
January 31, 1996. The Secretary, Ministry of Communications informed the
mission that the government expects to introduce new legislation (the National
Highway Safety Act) before the end of the calender year that will provide the
government legal authority to check and control vehicle overloading.
Contracting Industry Study.
19.   Only one proposal for the study was received on September 30, 1995. The
proposal is now under review by a committee and NHA's recommendations will be
sent to the Bank-by-Nov. 30, 1995. If the proposal is found unsatisfactory,
NHA would consider re-inviting proposals from short-listed firms. In view of
the FHP loan closing date; the mission indicated its no objection to NRA
, _..               .



6
financing expenditures related to this study beyond December 1995 from the
ongoing Transport Sector Project.
Road User Charges/Resource Generation Study.
20.   NRA awarded this study to a local Consultant in October 1994, following
clearance of the TOR and the draft Consultant's Contract by the Bank. An
Inception Report and the draft BOT policy has been reviewed and commented on
by the Bank. The overall duration of the study was to be seven months, but the
study is now six months late. The Bank has been informed that NHA and the
Consultant have agreed on a revised schedule to complete this work by December
1995. The findings of the study are expected to provide important inputs into
the preparation of the proposed Highway Rehabilitation Project.
Composite Schedule of Rates (CSR).
21.   The updating of the CSR has been completed by SAMPAK Consultants, the
consultant who prepared the original CSR. NHA has now agreed with the
consultants to train six NHA staff to operate the computer software package so
that the CSR can be updated by NHA whenever any of the indices related to
costs of labor, equipment and materials change. The mission was informed that
NRA is using the CSR to prepare construction and maintenance cost estimates
and to check the bids received for civil works.
Reallocations/Special Account and Disbursements.
22.   An amount of US$ 147.26 m has been disbursed as of September 30, 1995.
In response to NHA's request, the Loan has been reallocated between individual
categories, to fully utilize loan proceeds by the closing date. Recoveries
from NRA's Special Account (SA) are continuing in anticipation of the
approaching closing date and a sum of US$ 858,587 remains to be recovered. A
review of the SA by the mission indicates that NHA has withdrawn a sum of
about US$ 860,000 for ineligible expenditures. At the wrap-up meeting it was
agreed that NHA would either deposit this amount back into the SA, or provide
equivalent documentation for eligible expenditures incurred from its own
resources.
23.   The mission advised NHA that, following loan closure on December 31,
1995, the Bank will continue to honor withdrawal applications for expenditures
incurred (payments m2ade or payments due for goods, works and services which
have been provided prior to the closing date) which are received at the World
Bank, not later than four months after the closing date.
Audit Report.
24.   The legal agreement requires that the Borrower furnish to the Bank by
December 31 of each year, a certified copy of the audit report covering the
- -records-and-accounts in -respect of the Project (including the Statement of
Expenditures (SOEs) and the operation of the Special Account) for the year,
prepared by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank. NHA confirmed that
the-audit report for 1994/95 will be submitted to the Bank before this date.
Project Management Cell (PMC).
25.   The PMC consultant's contract services expired at end December 1994 and
the monitoring and cost control functions in the PMC are now being performed



*      ~~7
by NRA staff under the General Manager (Construction). The mission expressed
concern that the PMC consultant Advisor's Final Report was not yet available.
NRA has contacted the Consultant's Head Office to expedite completion of this
report.
Transfer of Completed Works
26.   The assets and facilities constructed under the project, consisting
primarily of the improved road network, have been identified and listed in
Attachment 3. The Taking-Over (Substantial Completion) Certificate has been
issued for most of the contracts and the Defects Liability (Maintenance)
Period has expired on a number of these contracts. However, to date, Defects
Liability (Maintenance) Certificates have been issued for only three
contracts. Several of the completed contracts (e.g. 6a, b; 7b etc.) have yet
to be handed over to the NHA maintenance division. A committee has been
established by NHA to undertake this function. The committee headed by
General Manager (Inspection) and consisting of representatives from regional
offices and headquarters has been charged with the responsibility of ensuring
that all contract close-out procedures are carried out prior to recommending
issuance of a Defects Liability (Maintenance) Certificate. The mission
requested NHA to provide the Bank by November 30, 1995, a schedule for the
issuance of outstanding Defects Liability Certificates, and to ensure prompt
handing over of completed/improved roads to the NRA maintenance division.
Project Implementation Completion Report
27.   As part of the Implementation Completion Report (ICR) preparation, NRA
is required to produce an acceptable Operation and maintenance plan that
ensures continued operation of the project works/facilities in accordance with
their operational specifications and sound engineering practices. The action
plan includes: (i) the required institutional arrangements, staffing needs,
and target dates by which these will be in place; (ii) estimates of the
additional operation and maintenance (O&M) costs, the source of funding, and
the manner in which funding will be phased into the maintenance schedule; and
(iii) the key physical, operational and financial indicators for monitoring
the performance of these assets and assessing their development impact.
28.   NRA provided the mission a draft of the operation plan at the wrap-up
(Attachment 4). The Bank will review the draft and provide any comments to NRA
following completion of the mission. It was agreed that NRA will finalize and
implement the operation plan following receipt of the Bank's comments.
29.   During the December 1994 mission, the Bank provided NRA the requirements
for the Implementation Completion Report (ICR) that has to be prepared on the
project closure. As part of the ICR procedures, the borrower is required to
submit its own evaluation report on project implementation and operation, the
ensuing costs and benefits, the performance of GOP and the Bank on their
..respective.obligations.under the loan agreement, the extent to which the
purposes of the loan were achieved and the lessons learned.  The mission has
explained these requirements to NRA for preparing its contribution to the ICR
process.
30.   To enable Bank staff to complete and distribute the ICR to the Bank's
Board within six months of the closing date, the mission proposed the
following schedule for ICR preparation which was confirmed by NHA at the wrap-
up meeting:



8

mission for the ICR (including traffic data,

II. TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT (Ln 3241-PAR)
(Aide Memoire No. 11)
1.    NHA is being assisted under the Transport Sector Project (TSP) in
reorganizing the Authority to a size, structure and level of professional
competence reflecting its responsibilities; and in the improvement of sections
of the national road network which are being now beyond normal Routine and
Periodic Maintenance. The Maintenance Backlog Reduction Program (MBRP) covers
those road sections which have deteriorated to a point where they require
reconstruction, rehabilitation or major improvement to return them to a
maintainable condition. The Resurfacing and Strengthening Program (RSP) is
intended to prevent the further deterioration of road pavements by providing
surface treatments or overlays.
2.    Although expenditures incurred on the RSP and minor MBRP components have
slowed somewhat since the last mission, the project is progressing generally
satisfactorily. However, Contract MBRP 7005 (Wadh-Surab) experienced
substantial flood damage during the unprecedented rains and floods in
Balochistan in June 1995. NHA has indicated to the mission that because of the
time needed to complete the additional works arising from this damage, GOP
will formally request the Bank for a one year extension of the closing date to
June 30, 1997. For the Bank to consider such a request, NHA should provide:
i)    a comprehensive consultants report on the flood event;
ii)   details of the resulting additional works including engineers estimates
of costs and the proposed implementation arrangements and schedule; and
iii) a financing plan and documentation to confirm the availability of
required counterpart funds.
NHA confirmed that this information would be provided by December 31, 1995.
3.    Most of the Actions Agreed during the previous mission have been carried
out. However, actions related to contract management matters, and adoption of
policies and procedures remain to be satisfactorily addressed. In addition the
mission drew NHA's attention to the urgent need to comply with the legal
covenants regarding: i) submission of agency audits, and ii) provision of
adequate counterpart funds. These are discussed further below.



10

Project Expenditure and Counterpart Funding
5.    Attachment 5 gives details of the project expenditures and counterpart
funding up to October 1995. An amount of Rs 500 m. (incl. Rs. 190 m. GOP
share) were allocated for TSP during FY95/96. Total expenditures of Rs. 95 m.
(incl. Rs. 36 m GOP share) were reported on the project during the current FY.
Project expenditures since Dec 1994 amount to about Rs 480 m indicating an
average monthly turnover of Rs 48 m. In addition, payments of about Rs. 130 m
on the major Wadh-Surab contract are expected shortly. Completion of the
balance works amounting to Rs 1083 m3, it will require additional counterpart
funds of Rs 436 m. The mission estimates that for the balance works to be
completed by the June 1996 closing date, total FY 95/96 allocations to the
project need to be doubled to about Rs 1000 m (incl. Rs 400 m as GOP share).
At the wrap-up meeting NRA confirmed that the required allocations will be
provided to ensure uninterrupted implementation of the project.
Civil Works.
MBRP and RSP Contracts.
6.    The physical achievements under the RSP and MBRP program have been
substantial. About 418.5 Km of the national highways in the four provinces
(150 Km on the N-5) have been taken up for repaving and strengthening under
--the-RSP--Rounds- I, II,-III and IV contracts and about 301 Km are being
rehabilitated under the MBRP Rounds I, II and IIIA contracts. In addition, 13
new bridges are being built and 16 other drainage related structures are being
rehabilitated.
Excluding 1995 Flood Damages.



11
7.    NHA informed the mission that 34 MBRP (LCB) contracts valued at Rs.612
million have been awarded, of which 10 have been completed and the rest are in
progress. Overall progress on the minor MBRP program is about 60%. The two
major MBRP (ICB) contracts are now 85% complete. These are discussed
separately below. A total of 76 RSP contracts valued at Rs. 934 million have
been awarded, including the 17 related to the 1992 flood damage. Of these, 52
RSP contracts have been completed and the rest are ongoing. Progress on the.
RSP program is now 81%.
8.    RSP and minor MBRP contract expenditures since Dec 1994 amount to about
Rs 172 m only, indicating a substantial slowing down in the previous pace of
implementation of the program. To complete the balance work remaining on these
programs by June 1996 requires a doubling of the recent pace of progress.
9.    The mission noted with concern that four contracts in RSP Round III and
another tour in Round IIIA have encountered significant completion delays (of
the order of six months). Also two of the MBRP Round II contracts (7006 and
7012) are reportedly still in progress although the completion dates expired
over 15 months ago. Causes for delayed completion apparently include a
combination of poor contractor performance, and cumbersome contract
administration and payment procedures. The mission has requested NHA to
expedite any pending contractual administration decisions, closely scrutinize
the minor MBRP/RSP contract portfolio and promptly apply contractual remedies
in case of non-performing contractors, including termination if appropriate.
10.   Attachment 5 shows balance work remaining to be completed on the ongoing
RSP contracts. The mission noted that pace of implementation of some of the
earlier contracts needs to be substantially increased to achieve completion by
June 1996. NHA also needs to ensure timely completion of the recently awarded
RSP Round IV and MBRP IIIA contracts. NHA has been requested to provide the
Bank with a report by November 30, 1995, on the actions taken in regard to the
above matters. It was agreed that future TSP monthly progress reports would
flag contracts with significant delays, and indicate remedial actions.
Major MBRP Projects.
11.   Two major MBRP contracts have been awarded following ICB procedures.
Contract MBRP - 7004 for the construction of an additional carriageway on a 30
Km section of the N-5 between Sahiwal and Okara was awarded to ECLC (PVT) Ltd.
(Pakistan). Work started in July 1992. The original contract completion date
of July 19, 1994 has now been extended three times to December 31, 1995.
Progress is only about 57%. Despite NRA's efforts to expedite completion by
establishing a jointly managed escrow account, progress continues to fall
behind. The Supervisory consultant has estimated that at least a further 12
months are needed to complete the contract. The mission agrees that this
contract is unlikely to be completed by June 1996 under the present
arrangements. NRA assured the mission that the contract is being closely
monitored, and agreed that contractual remedies would be applied if progress
of about 70% of the contract value is not achieved by December 1995. The
mission strongly recommends that a detailed cash flow and direct cost analysis
be done to ascertain whether the amount remaining in the contract is
sufficient to cover actual costs to complete the works under the escrow
arrangement.
12.   Supervision is now being done by NESPAK following the completion of the
services provided by Kampsax on December 31, 1994. NESPAK is presently the
Engineer on the contract, however their role as Engineer is being compromised,



12
and they appear to have been bypassed on a number of contractual matters,
including the latest decision on time extension. The mission requested NHA to
review this matter, and to clarify the future role of the Consultant.
13.   Contract MBRP 7005 for the rehabilitation of about 160 Km of the N-25
between Wadh and Surab was awarded to M/S Joannou & Paraskevaides (Overseas)
Ltd. (Cyprus) in June 1992. Progress is estimated at about 90%. About 147 Km
of the road has now been opened to traffic and about 11 Km remain. Substantial
completion (excluding 1995 flood damages) is expected by early December 1995.
14.   During the site visit the mission inspected the works, including the
recent flood damages particularly the major damage locations. Preliminary
details of the flood damage are contained in the July and August 1995 monthly
progress reports. Consultants have been appointed by NHA to re-examine the
adequacy of the cross drainage structures. They are currently preparing a
detailed hydrological analysis of the related catchments. Preliminary findings
indicate that additional culverts, bridges and protective works will be
needed. Initial estimates provided to the mission are as follows:
a)    Flood damage restoration work:        Rs  60 m
b)    Four major bridge works:              Rs 155 m
(Km. 356, 410, 457 and 469)
c)    Additional culverts and protective  Rs 160 m
Total       Rs 375 m
These costs are currently being finalized by NHA. The designs (including
hydrological analysis) and engineer's estimates for items (a) and (b) are
expected to be available by Nov. 30, 1995 and for (c) by Jan 31, 1996.
15.   The mission noted that at two bridge locations, Km 356 and Km 457, it
may be possible to realign the road to avoid completely the river channels now
causing the problems. Such realignments could be less costly than the bridge
solutions being considered and would solve permanently the present drainage
problem at these two locations, in addition to avoiding heavy maintenance
costs in future. NHA agreed to do a comparative analysis on the proposals for
these two locations in order to identify the best engineering and most cost
effective solutions for the problems before the designs are finalized.
16.   The construction is being supervised by Kampsax (Denmark) in association
with A. A. Associates (Pakistan). Kampsax are providing an expatriate CRE at
the site. Although NHA has designated Kampsax as the "Engineer', the firm had
refused to accept this role from January 1995 citing their agreement with NHA
under Addendum No.6. NHA confirmed that this matter has now been mutually
resolved, and Kampsax had agreed to accept the role of the "Engineer" under
Addendum No.7.
17.   The-mission noted with concern that a number of contract administration
related-issues remain unattended to-on this contract. During the field visit
the -mission's attention was drawn to the following: (i) Confusion about who is
the "Engineer" on the contract; (ii) What are the powers of the Engineer?;
(iii) De.lays rin payment of Interim payment certificates (IPCs) - four IPCs
currently outstanding, two for more than sixty days; (iv) Delays in issuing
Certificates of Substantial Completion - one is outstanding for the past 18
month;---(v)-Delays in finalizing extensions of Contract Period; (vi) Delays in
the issuing of Variation orders; and (vii) Multiple often conflicting
instructions-from various NHA staff, as the Employer's representatives. These
matters have been brought to the attention of NRA senior management, and the



13
mission has been assured that necessary clarifications on the above will
issued to all concerned by Nov. 30, 1995 and a copy provided to the Bank for
information. The mission expects that such confusion will be avoided in
future, when NHA adopts and implements the Policies and Procedures and
Construction Administration Manuals (Ref. para 29 below).
18.   The contract for KOSHAK Bridge  was started in April 1993 and was
scheduled for completion by December 1994. The bridge structures and approach
embankments have now been completed. Agreement has been reached that J & P,
the main road contractor will complete the pavement work as a subcontractor.
The 1995 monsoon floods damaged certain sections of the bridge approaches.
These have now been repaired by the contractor. The existing right Guidebund
is being extended by about 300m under a variation to the contract, to minimize
the risk of similar damage during future floods. The consultants, A. A.
Associates are supervising the work.
Accounting Assistance and Financial System Strenathening.
19.   The Accounting Assistance Scheme (Phase 1) is well under way, the
consultants having mobilized their team in July 1995. The assignment has two
main objectives: i) the preparation of financial statements for the years
ended 30 June 1993, 1994 and 1995; and ii) the development and implementation
of revised accounting policies and a revised system using a standard general
accounting computer package.
20.   The information required for compiling the financial statements are not
readily present at NHA's Head Office. The consultants have developed standard
formats for collection of required information which were circulated by NHA to
all locations. However no response has been received to date. It was agreed
that NHA would take steps to expedite the provision of information which
should be made available to the consultants no later than November 21, 1995.
As a result of the lack of response, the consultants have been unable to
commit a firm schedule for the preparation of financial statements. It was
agreed that a detailed review of the status of information preparation would
be carried out and a detailed schedule provided to the Bank by November 30,
1995.
21.   Progress on the second aspect is satisfactory.  Revised accounting
guidelines and procedures have been developed and are under implementation, as
is the standard accounting system at the Head Office. This will be a
comprehensive system, in accordance with modern international accounting
standards, that also caters for all subsidiary information required by other
government agencies.
Disbursements, Special Account and Reallocation
22.   A total of US$ 53.5 m has been disbursed on the Highway component, as of
September 30, 1995. However, the mission's review indicates that NHA has not
managed the SA on this project in accordance with the agreed procedures. A
number of payments have been made by the NHA, without no objection from the
Bank. A sum of USS 413,439 remains to be refunded to the SA by NHA on account
of the ineligible expenditures. The mission requested immediate action on this
matter.
23.   Since the closing date for this project is June 30, 1996, the normal
recovery of the SA would start from January next. The total amount to be
recovered is currently US$ 6,878,466.



14
24.   The Bank has recently received a request from EAD for the reallocation
of US$ 20 m from the unutilized railway component of the TSP, to the MBRP
program. This request was broadly discussed with NHA during the mission. NHA
has been asked to provide a detailed proposal for utilization of the
reallocation proceeds in accordance with the original project objectives, and
confirmation about the availability of necessary counterpart funds, for the
Bank to respond to this request.
TSP Audits:
25.   The Loan Agreement requires that NHA furnish to the Bank by December 31
of each year certified copies of its financial statements for the year
(including records and accounts of its Special Account) duly audited by
independent auditors acceptable to the Bank. NHA confirmed that audited
project accounts for FY94/95 will be provided to the Bank by this date. Also
substantial progress has been made under the guidance of NHA Financial
Adviser, towards developing auditable agency accounts. NHA's consolidated
financial statements from July 1990 to June 1995 have recently been prepared.
Based on these documents, the Accounting Assistance Study consultants will
carry out reconciliations and field verifications. These will then be
submitted for independent audit. In view of the substantial work that remains
to be done to finalize these accounts, the mission agreed with NHA that the
audited agency wide accounts would be submitted by March 31, 1996.
Institutional Strengthening of NHA.
26.   One of the principal objectives of the Transport Sector Project was to
assist in reorganizing the NHB (the predecessor to NHA) to a size, structure
and level of professional competence reflecting its responsibilities for the
planning, construction, maintenance, operation and administration of the
National Highway System. Annex 2.2 of the TSP Staff Appraisal Report spells
out the Action Plan for NHB Institutional Strengthening. Key elements of the
Plan included: a) Reorganization of NHA according to a functional Structure;
b) Introduction of Policies and Procedures appropriate to the new
organization; and c) Placement of the most competent and best qualified
persons available in key positions, in an open and transparent manner.
27.   The NHA was established through the NHA Act in 1991. However, progress
on the institutional strengthening measures agreed at project appraisal,
remained slow until this year. In early 1995, the National Highway Council
approved the NHA organizational structure, Service Rules, and Delegation of
Financial Powers. The majority of NHA staff who were previously working on
work-charge have now been regularized, and the Authority has issued job
descriptions for all approved posts in September. Promotion cases of General
Managers and staff in BS-1 to 16 have been finalized, while the remaining (BS
18, 19) are expected by end of November 1995. These developments have already
led to a visible improvement in general staff morale.
Policies & Procedures
28.   The mission's discussions with NHA central and regional staff, including
general managers, project directors, contractors and consultants, indicate
that serious bottlenecks remain in the area of construction and contract
administration. This is also borne out by frequent delays in payment of IPCs,
consultants bills, issuance of variation orders, Substantial Completion and
Maintenance Certificates, physical hand-over of project sites to contractors,



15
etc.  There is some confusion about the role and responsibility of the
"Engineer" and the "Employer" as defined in the contracts, and the role and
responsibility of the Project Directors, Regional General Managers and Central
NRA staff in regard to contract implementation. The delays in payments and
the release of retention moneys due to the contractors, carry the risk of
claims and resulting increases in contract cost. They also hurt NHA's
reputation and international standing as a major Employer of public works in
this region, and discourage the participation by good professional firms and
contractors in NHA's future works as well as potential private investors in
the highway sub-sector.
29.   In order to address the above longstanding concerns, it was agreed
during the Feb 1994 mission that NHA would set up a committee to review the
Policy and Procedures Manual and Construction Administration Manual prepared
by NHA's consultants following a major study in 1990, adapt them as necessary
in light of current NHA structure, and arrange for their adoption by April
1994. This target date was revised to March 1995 during the previous
supervision mission. There has been some further slippage, but the mission was
pleased to learn that Planning, Finance and Operations have now completed
their review of the Policy and Procedures Manual, and a consolidated draft of
the updated Manual (with changes highlighted) will be provided to the Bank by
November 30, 1995. The manual would be adopted by December 31, 1995. NRA's
review of the Construction Administration Manual remains to be undertaken. It
was agreed that an updated version of this manual would be provided to the
Bank by January 30, 1996 and that NRA will arrange seminars at Islamabad, and
in the regions during the first half of 1996 to disseminate the procedures and
responsibilities detailed in these two manuals, following their adoption.
30. The mission asked NRHA to ensure that recent developments, including the
requirements of the Bank's revised (Jan 1995) Procurement Guidelines and
latest Standard Bidding Documents are reflected in the revised manuals. NHA
agreed to constitute a committee charged with reviewing and updating these
manuals at regular intervals to ensure that they remain current with new
developments. The committee would be headed by Member Administration.
31.   The present level of office and field review of the consultants' designs
by NHA also needs substantial strengthening. Some of the designs examined by
the mission were clearly inadequate and not to the professional standards
needed for such important investments. The mission was informed that NHA is
currently in the process of inducting additional staff with appropriate design
background and experience into its Planning Wing for this purpose.
32.   The mission also suggest that NRA consider instituting a system whereby
representatives from Planning, Construction and Maintenance jointly sign-off
on a consultants design/drawings before these are issued for construct;on.
This will minimize the risk of subsequent changes/variations during
construction, facilitate takeover of the completed works by maintenance staff
on completion, and most importantly provide a feedback mechanism for
incorporating the lessons learned during project implementation into future
projects.
Technical Assistance
33.   NHA and the Bank have held discussions during the past several months on
various forms of Technical Assistance, particularly with reference to
facilitating Private Sector Participation in the highway sector. The Bank has
offered to finance a long term local consultant under the project, to work as



16
a private sector finance advisor within NHA's Private Sector Finance Cell. NHA
has recently advertised for a suitable candidate for this position. In
addition the Bank has offered to provide international experts/ consultants to
assist in NRA's PS initiatives, possibly financed through Trust Funds (TF).
34.   During the mission, the Chairman NRA requested the Bank for: i) short-
term expert assistance in connection with its negotiations with a private
developer for the Implementation Agreement for the Karachi Northern Bypass,
where a LOI has already been issued; and ii) developing suitable RFP documents
for inviting private concessionaires to bid for operation of the Lahore-
Islamabad Motorway project, which is scheduled for completion by June 1997.
35. At the wrap-up meeting NHRA provided the mission a copy of the basic
project proposal for the Northern Bypass. The mission indicated that it would
consult with Bank headquarters to see how appropriate short-term expert
inputs/advice can best be provided to NHA. With regard to the Lahore-
Islamabad Motorway, further discussions will be held with NHA staff, to
develop draft TOR for NHA's review and agreement, before a Trust Fund request
can be processed possibly in March/April 1996.
Agreed Actions

III. PROPOSED HIGHWAY REHABILITATION PROJECT
1.    The mission discussed the draft concept paper for the proposed Highway
Rehabilitation Project (Attachment 6) with the officials of the National
Highway Authority, and the ministries of Planning, Finance, Communications and
the Economic Affairs Division. There was general agreement on the major sub-
sectoral issues raised in the paper, and the broad policy principles and
project components proposed for the future project. The mission also met with
the OECF Resident Representative to exchange views on the policy issues facing
the sector and to explore possible OECF interest in the proposed project.
2.    The NHA gave the mission a comprehensive technical presentation on the
current pavement condition of the N-5 existing carriageway (Attachment 7), and
an initial identification of the sections requiring reconstruction and
overlays. The presentation provided an impressive demonstration by NHA of the
usefulness and application of NHA's evolving Pavement Management System and of
the enhanced in-house capabilities and performance of the NHA staff. NKA's
presentation highlighted the distressing condition of the N-5 existing
carriageway pavement and showed that of the 1624 Km length surveyed about 1160
Km (71%) had a "Remaining Service Life" of less than 3 years. A full report of
the work done to date would be provided to the Bank by Dec.31, 1995.
3.    It was agreed that a bridge component will be added to NHA's list of
physical works, and this will be developed and refined during the course of
project preparation. The NHA requested the Bank to consider financing 70% of
the cost of rehabilitation and overlay works, in line with the existing MBR?
program. The mission explained that the overall size of the project and the
Bank's share of the physical works would be determined to fit the extent of
external financing and counterpart funds that are available.
4.    NHA has al-ready constituted a project preparation team for the proposed
project, and Mr. Uzair Fazil, GM(Maint) has been designated as the team
leader. Preparatory work would require financial, institutional and technical
studies which were broadly discussed with NHA. Detailed TOR for these studies
will be developed by NHA in consultation with the Bank during the next few
months. Some funds are available under the Transport Sector Project which may
initially be used to finance this preparatory work. NHA would consider use of
PPF funds for any additional financing that may be needed. The Bank would also
explore the possibility of identifying Trust Funds for some of the policy
related work.
5.    The mission discussed with NHA the Bank's project cycle and processing
requirements and agreed on a provisional project preparation and
implementation schedule (Attachment 8). The schedule indicates key steps both
in the Borrower's and the Bank's project cycle. The mission clarified that,
within the Bank, the final decision on the future processing of the project,
its scope and possible components, will be made following the I..itial
Executive Project Summary (IEPS) review meeting scheduled for January 1996.
6.    The Secretary, Ministry of Communications informed the mission that the
government expects to complete a large number of the ongoing NHA projects by
mid-1997. He confirmed the government's agreement to the principle of
introducing road user charges, and requested the Bank to advance the
processing schedule of the proposed project to the extent possible.



18
7.    The mission informed NHA that the following actions are necessary to
facilitate further processing of the project:
(i)   Completion of the Resource Mobilization Study. NRA should provide the
Bank with a copy of the draft final report by December 31, 1995.
(ii)  Adoption by NHA of the updated Policies and Procedures Manual by
December 31, 1995.

FOURTH IBRD HIGHWAY PROJECT

5.    OPERATTON AND MATNTENANCE PLAN
The following describes the NHA's strategy on operation and maintenance of
completed projects.
MATNTENANCE OF ROAD NETWORK
The highway system of a country is an index of its development both in terrms of
the role it can play in the economic life of that country, and in terms of the
investment made in creating the highway network. It is, therefore, important to
ailocate and expend further resources in a judicious manner in the maintenance of
the system.
In a general sense, the termn highway maintenance encompasses not only pavement
but also the associated structures, drainage and ancillary ground works. The major
part of any maintenance program, however, is usually the protection and
rectification of defects in the pavement surface. NTHA shall mnaintain the Fourth
Highway Project in accordance with its already established maintenance procedures
which are given below:
a.    ROUTTNE MAINTENANCE
Those ordinary maintenance activities which are required once or more
times each year to preserve pavements, structures, shoulders and drains
against the combined effects of traffic, climate and topography. For
example; filling of potholes, patches using surface treatment/asphalT
concrete bringing shoulders to required level, clearing of drains/culverts,
renewal of paint application etc.
b.    PERTODIC MAINTENANCE
Includes those ordinary maintenance activities which are required once
every second or more years only to restore the pavements, structures,
shoulders, drains and verges to the conditions they were in at the time of
constuction, or subsequent reconstruction. For example; minor overlays
in surface dressing, major overlays in asphaltic concrete on those sections
not requiring the base structure to be repaired, shoulder leveling, minor
bridge/culvert/drain repairs etc.



Attachment-4
Page 3 of 15
c.    EMERGENCY MAINTENANCE
Emergency Mainienance is defined as work needed under emergent
conditions usually such situation arrives after rain fall, floods & damage to
the road due to other adversaries and traffic restoration is essential as an
emergency work. Apart from this if same bridge structure or culvert is
damaged, diversion is needed under emergency. General Manager
(Regional) is authorized to mobilize any contractor available for the Routine
Maintenance or Periodic Maintenance close to the site preferably and if he
does not respond then any other contractor is asked to mobilize under
emergency. The financial limit is upto Rs 1 million, in case the emergent
work- scope exceed Rs I million, approval from Chairman NHA is obtained
through fax and formalities completed accordingly regarding bids invitation
at 3 days or 7 days notice.
d.    MANTENANCE BACKLOG REDUCTION
Rehabilitation is those extraordinary maintenance activities which may be
required intermittently to catch up with any backlog of scheduled routine
and periodic maintenance activities. For example; major pavement and
structural works, like complete strengthening of bases, sub-bases and
overlaying. Rebuilding of very badly damaged or collapsed structures.
e.    IMPROVEMENTS
Improvements are those extraordinary maintenance (minor improvement),or
development (major improvement) activities, which may be required
intermittently to strengthen or widen those roads and strUCtures which have
been rendered under-designed due to increases in traffic or axle loads. This
can also involve modifying the geometric layout of the road to suit the
modern conditions.
6.    EVALUATTON OF STAFF REOUTREMENT
The Organization Chart of the Maintenance Staff is attached as Annexure B(1),
B(2), B(3) & B(4) on the following pages. This staffing as per Organization Chan
will look after the projects of FHP also and there is no separate additional
requirement of addidonal staff.

a.    JOB DESCRIPTTON (REGIONAL MAINTENANCE STAFF)
DTRECTOR (MATNTENANCE)
*     To inform GM(Region) with all maintenance problems.
*     To supervise maintenance works within jurisdiction.
*     To maintain full record of maintenance works and payments
thereon.
*     To ensure that all National Highways are in proper
conditions and there is no hindrance for traffic.
*     To prepare and submit progress reports on maintenance
works as directed by the Seniors.
*     To ensure quality control at site.
*     To ensure proper attendance of Consultants/Contractors and
NHA staff at site.
*     To ensure completion of maintenance works within the
scheduled time and approved estimates.
*     To ensure proper utilization of machinery at site as per
contract conditions.
*     To ensure supply of material at site in due course of time, so
that work may not suffer.
*     To ensure payment of bills to consultants/contractors as early
as possible after its submission.
*     To check the completion  of MBs for claims by
Contractors/Consultants.
*   To prepare replies on audit observations pertaining to
respective area of maintenance work.

jurisdiction.

routine and periodic maintenance contracts.
*     To maintain full record of maintenance works and payments
thereon.
*     To ensure that all National Highways are in proper
conditions and there is no hindrance for traffic.
*     To prepare and submit progress reports on maintenance
works as directed by the Seniors.

*     To prepare replies on audit observations pertaining to
respective area of maintenance work.
AsiTsTANT DIRECTOR (MATNTENANCE)
*     To be physically present/supervise the maintenance works of the
area allocated to them.
*     To ensure quality and completion of work in due course of time.
*     To check/certify the claims of consultants and contractors.
*     To ensure punctuality of NHA/Contractors staff at site.
*     To ensure proper functioning of National Highway under his
supervision.
*     To ensure that no one is trying for illegal encroachments in the right

8.    TECHNICAL ARRANGEMENTS
National Highway Authority has established the Pavement Monitoring System
which will be carrying out the road condition survey to determine the Maintenance
Intervention Level (MIL) score of the whole network including the sections that will
be added under the FHP and will serve the basis to identify the nature of
maintenance contract requirements.
The Maintenance Intervention Levels are numerical ratings, these ratings are placed
upon a pavement defect and its severity. The defects and severity categories have
all been developed by the Transport Research Laboratory in the United Kingdom
based upon years of empirical research in many countries with many varied types of
road condition. All the ratings are objective measurements and rule out disparities of
what constitutes a maintenance problem.
To this physical assessment of the pavement condition is added an environment
rating which is based upon the local conditions of climate, traffic volumes and
predominant geotechnical features such as C.B.R of the predominant subgrade.
By building up an aggregated "score" for each 5 km link of the network, it is
possible to favorably compare one link to another (given the same starting
parameters) and simply target the maintenance money according to the scores.
The range of scores applying to the National Highway network of Pakistan are as
follows:
>70      This road needs reconstruction/rehabilitation. Only safery related
maintenance should be carried out.
60-69    The road pavement is in need of improvement. Major periodic overlays
should be programmed.
50-59    A road segment with this rating needs localized periodic maintenance.
40-49    This road segment needs routine maintenance.
>40      This section of road needs routine maintenance. It should be given
attention only as budgets permit.
9



Attachment-4
Pase 13 of 15
ML Presents limitless opportunities for use. I t can be used under a wide variery of
conditions. The scoring parameters can be amended to suit the development of the
network as interventions become effective.
Deputy Directors Maintenance Field Unit is responsible for the subject sections
while submitting the estimated quantities for the maintenance activities to be
required on above sections as per normal yearly maintenance program and
necessary adjustment will be carried out as per availability of the budgeL

consideration. The Ministry of Finance after detailed review, allocates the budget
on yearly basis. Normally it has been observed that allocation is about 50% of the
total demand. Further NHA will take up the case with Ministry of Communications

11.   KEY OPERATIONAL AND FINANCIAL TNDTCATORS
The success or failure of any infrastructure project can be gauged from various
operational and financial indicators which provide the funding Agencies and the
Executing Agency with feed back about success of the project over the design life.
It is suggested that the following indicators would be used to monitor the road
completed under the World Bank assisted Fourth Highway Project.                      t
11 



Attachment-4
Page 15 of 15
a.    ROAD CONDITION INDICATOR
The assessment of the road condition over the design life of the completed
facility is a positive indicator as to the successful completion of the project
in accordance with design and specification. In the NHA road condition
Operational Indicators are Maintenance Intervention Levels (MIL) which
take various factors into account and ranks the road condition in a scale of I
to 100 which shows deteriorating road condition as the score increase.
However, NHA is now developing a Pavement Management System which
would take factors like rutting, cracking, roughness, traffic, structural
response (deterrnined with FWD), drainage, raveling and pot holes. In the
PMS a scoring scale of zero to hundred will be used and the concept of
remaining service life will be used for each individual factor.
b.    TRAFFIC TNIDCATOR
The percentage annual increase in traffic over and above the normal growth
rate can be a reliable indicator towards the acceptance of the completed road
as being a better facility than alternately available routes. Monitoring of the
indicators will be carried at the end of each year to assess the Operation
Plan.
C.    PERCENTAGE OF FUNDIN(G FOR MAINTENANCE OF THE
COMPLETED FACTLITIES
Lower maintenance needs of any road section is a fair indicator of the
condition of a road with assumption that a road in good condition w ill need
lesser maintenance needs and thus lesser expenditure as compared to
altemate roads in the network.

PAIaSTAN
ProDosed Hiehwav Rehabilitation Proiect
Concept Paper - Discussion Draft
October 12, 1995
Countrv/Sector Backaround.
1.     The transport and communications sector accounts for about 10% of Pakistan's GDP. During the
1980s demand for infrastructure grew more rapidly than GDP or population, while public sector
investment in infastructure declined due to budgetary constraints. The adverse impact of past under
investment has been accentuated by inadequate recurrent expenditures on maintenance. As a result, severe
bottlenecks have developed in the transportation system, and levels of service remain low. It is estimated
that inadequate transport infrastructure increases transport costs in Pakistan by up to 50%, and is
estimated to cost the economy about USS 0.5 billion annually.
2.     Pakistan's primary traffic artery is the 1700 km Karachi-Lahore-Peshawar corridor. The
corridor's transport system serves domestic needs, and also links Punjab with international markets
through the Karachi area ports. About 60% of the port traffic moves to and from Punjab along the
corridor. Domestic freight traffic has been gro'hing at an annual rate of 5% during the past 25 years, and
the incremental traffic has been carried by the trucking industry. Rail traffic has been stagnant. Currently
over 85% of the inland passenger and freight traffic is carried by road.
3.     The 6600 km long National Highway System accounts for less than 4% of the overall road
network, but carries two-thirds [?I of the total traffic. It's main artery, the 1760 Ian long N-5 highway
along the Karachi-Lahore-Peshawar corridor carries the bulk of the country's inter-city traffic. Previous
Bank operations (Highways m and IV), have assisted in upgrading sections of this highway from two to
four lanes, by constructing about 500 Kmn of additional carraigeway. During 1991-93 GOP embarked on a
major highway expansion program that included contracts for the construction of the additional
carraigeway for most of the remaining N-5 sections. These GOP financed contracts are expected to be
completed by 1996-97.
4.     Although a substantial section of the N-5 is now operating as a dual carraigeway, the original
carraigeway is showing sevqre signs of distress in several sections . About 200 Km of this original
carraigeway have recently been rehabilitated under Highways IV. During 1994, GOP formally requested
the Banlk for assistance with reconstruction of the remaining length of existing carraigeway along the N-5.
5.     NHA's annual development expenditures increased over fivefold between FY91 and FY93 and
have stabilized around Rs 10-11 billion annually over the past three years. However, these have not been
matched by corresponding real increases in NHA's annual maintenance allocations, which have stagnated
at less than Rs 0.5 billion. The agency is currently spending barely 6% of its total budgel on routine and
periodic maintenance, while it is estimated that the shortfall in NHA's annual maintenance expenditures
has increased from 50% to 65% of network needs in the three year period up to FY95. Between 1989 and
1993 the proportion of the National Highway network in good condition (i.e. requiring only routine
maintenance) dropped sharply from 50% to 35%. This position is likely to worsen, as a result of normal
traffic growth, greater pavement damage caused by a significant increase in average axle loads, and a
continuming modal shift in freight transportation from road to rail, unless current fiscal constraints limiting
substantial increases in maintenance funding can be overcome.
6.     The Bank assisted Transport Sector Project included covenants on increased annual funding
levels for highway maintenance as part of NHA's CIMP. Although these expenditure targets were broadly
achieved, thy were outstripped by actual network requirements on account of the factors listed above.
7.     Initial estimnates indicate that total revenues mobilized from road users (taxes, duties, license fees,
tolls, etc.) over the preceding three year period roughly equaled total federal and provincial highway
sector expenditures. This indicates that road user charges, when adjusted for general revenue taxes, are
less than highway sector expenditures.



Attachment-6
Page 3 of 8
Proposed Highway Rehabilitation Project                                     October 12, 1955
(                     Strategv and Needs
8.     Increased resource mobilization from road users is clearly needed to finance NHA's maintenance
expenditures to a level required to preserve the network. While a Resource Mobilization study
commissioned under Highways XV is expected to provide detailed information shortly, it is anticipated that
an earmarked surcharge of the order of 15% on transport fuels can for example provide sufficient
revenues to pay for the annual routine and periodic maintenance needs of the National Highway Network.
Such an increase in user charges should be politically acceptable, if it is clearly linked through a
transparent mechanism to increased road maintenance expenditures, and strengthened accountability in
highway agency operations. The additional revenues when placed in a dedicated Road Fund, can provide
a steady source of financing for NHA's operation and maintenance expenditures, that is independent of the
vagaries of the federal budget.
9.     NHA's sole reliance on the federal budget for financial support can also be minimized, and the
development of a modem highway system accelerated by encouraging Private Sector Participation (PSP)
in highway investments and operations. This requires the introduction of a supportive legal, financial and
regulatory framework; as well as building up capacity within the agency to promote PSP. Private
concessions for operating NHA's existing assets like the Lahore-Islamabad Motorway and the Lahore
Bypass etc. as tolled facilities, can minimize further public sector investments in these works, generate
additional revenues and lead to modern and efficient operation of these major facilities.
10.    Carefully prioritized public sector highway investments wvill be required for the NHA network in
the future, to maximize benefits in a period where public sector development funds are limited and the
PSDP is facing severe macro economic constraints. These could include selective expansion of network
capacity, pavement strengthening to cater for growing traffic loads, improving ride quality etc., primarily
along Pakistan's high densitv traffic corridors.
Proiect Obiectives
II.    The project objectives are:
a.     Increased resource mobilization from road users, promoting efficient use of the highway
network and discouraging vehicle overloading [By adjusting the level and structure of user
charges and by enforcement of axle load control].
b.     Creation of an earmarked source of additional revenue for funding NHA's routine
operation and maintenance functions. Financial strengthening of the agency, and progressively
declining dependence on budgetary support.
c.     Improving the accountability in NHA's operations [making agency performance more
transparent through the use of explicit performance indicators; linking highway maintenance
expenditures to payments made by road users; and wider stakeholder participation in
management/ utilization of the Road Fund resources]
d.     Promoting Private Sector Participation in highway investments/operations.
e.     Encouraging growth, reducing tansportation costs and increasing competitiveness of
the country's exports. [Through capacity expansion and improved riding quality for the highest
priority sections of the National Highway System, primarily N-5].
-2-



*                                                                                         Attachment-6
Page 4 of 8
Proposed Highway Rehabilitation Project                                     October 12, 1955
f.     Development of a modern highway sector in Pakistan. [Capacity building and
institutional strengthening of NHA. Implementing measures to reduce roadside friction
particularly along bypasses; promote highway safety; strengthen the domestic highway
construction industry, develop capacity of the domestic consultant firms etc.]
Proiect Description
12.    Achievement of the project objectives will be ensured by reaching agreement to specific up-front
actions in the following areas:
a.     Appropriate adjustments in the current level and structure of road user charges.
b.     Earmarking of a portion of revenues from road users to finance NHA's routine annual
operation and maintenance (O&M) costs.
c.     Setting up of a Road Fund jointly managed by a Board comprising of government
nominees and Road User Association representatives. The Fund to be used for financing only
designated NHA O&M expenditures.
d.     Necessary legislation for axle load control.
C.     Putting in place of a supportive policy and regulatory framework for PSP. Award of
concessions for the operation and maintenance of NHA's rnajor revenue generating assets
(Lahore-Islamabad motorway, Lahore Bypass etc.) as tolled facilities by the private sector.
13.    Project components could consist of:
a.     Annual routine and periodic maintenance of the N-5 for the duration of the project,
determined on the basis of NHA's pavement/maintenance management svstem, financed entirely
from the proposed Road Fund.
b.     A phased program of pavement strengthening for selected sections of the N-5 exissung
carraigeway that are structurally sound, financed partly from the Road Fund, and partly [on a
declining annual basis i.e. 60,50,40,30,200/%] by IBRD etc.
c.     Reconstruction of a portion of the N-5 original carraigeway (including structures)
selected on the basis of traffic levels, existing pavement condition and other related factors,
financed partly from the NHA PSDP and partly by 1BRD (and possibly other cofinanciers) .
d.     A focused technical assistance program to assist the NHA PSFinancing cell in
developing a portfolio of bankable private sector highway investments/concessions, and
preparation of RFPs, with the aim of achieving at least one or two signed concession agreements
within an 18 month span.
e.     Technical assistance, capacity building and implementation support for upgrading
NHA's accounting and financial systems; management of the Road Fund and periodic review of
the level of user charges; implementing transparent and streamlined contract administration
procedures; strengthening in-country pavement management and maintenance capabilities;
developing country specific pavement design procedures and material specifications, etc.

Proposed Highway Rehabilitation Project                                      October 12, 1955
( -                   Proiect Costs & Financing
14.    Reliable estimates of total cost of upgrading the existing N-S carraigeway require comprehensive
studies and field investigations, including pavement condition surveys and prehminary designs. However
some indicative cost estimates based on the general network condition obtained through the MII, system
introduced under Highways IV, are attached in Table 1.
1S.    If, for the sake of initial broad estimates, it is assumed that of the total length of N-S, 500 km
(28%) require pavement strengthening, and 600 km require total reconstruction, then a project covering
maintenance and upgrading of the entire N-S over a five year period would currently cost around PRs 29
billion (USS 900 mnillion). Undertaling such a project in a single phase (under the financing Plan
indicated in Case 1), would require external financing of the order of PRs 13.5 billion (USS420 nillion)
i.e. 47% of total project costs. The annual expenditure levels for such a project are under PRs 6 billion, i.e.
about 45% of NHA's planned FY96 expenditures.
16.    Case E1 descnbes a more flexible phased approach to upgrading of N-S. The overall scope of work
in Phase I is scaled down to match the availability of external financing and counterpart funds. The
project scope is curtailed to include only limited segments of the highway requiring total reconstruction,
while the other components remain unchanged. If, for example, only 300 Km of highway reconstruction
are included, the total project costs become PRs 18 billion (USS 560 mnillion). In this case external
financing is required to finance about PRs 7.7 billion (USS 240 mnillion), i.e. 43% of total project costs.
17.    The exact size of the proposed project and the possible extent of external financing would be
established during the course of project preparation. IBRD financing of up to USS 200 million may be
possible for such an operation. Interest of other donors in co-financing the project remains to be explored.
18.    Initial estimates indicate that the N-5 may require about Rs 0.75 billion in annual maintenance
costs for year 1. Since maintenance of N-5 constitutes about half of the entire National Highway network
maintenance costs, the Road Fund would need to generate around PRs 1.5 billion in year I of the project,
and about PRs 2.5 billion by year 6 when the project closes, and NHA becomes responsible for financing
the full maintenance costs of N-5 from its own resources.
Preparation and Implementation
19.    Project preparation will be used to strengthen the in-country project planning and development
capacity. In order to ensure ownership, the borrower (NHAIGOP) will be asked to take the lead in
preparation, using the assistance of a management consultant, if necessary. The Bank will contribute to
the preparation effort by participating in the policy dialogue with GOP, and by assisting with
development/ preparation of the more innovative aspects of the project (Road Fund/ earmarking / PSP
etc.) drawing on the successful application of these concepts elsewhere in the world.
20.    Technical assistance wiU be required in the form of consultant inputs for specific preparation
tasks. Limited uncommitted funds are available under the ongoing TSP [closing mid-19961, some of
which may be reallocated for this purpose. However, additional resources (PIID trust funds or PPF) will
be required for project preparation. The objective will be to finalize engineeritg designs and design of the
Technical Assistance for implementation before Appraisal, and bidding documents for the first two vears
of project implementation prior to project negotiations.
21.    Implementation of the policy components of the project will require the agreement of the
borrower's federal ministries of Finance, Planning, Communications and Law etc. Implementatiun of the
project's physical components and studies iwill be through the National Highs ay Authority.
.4.



Attachment-6
Page 6 of 8
Proposed Highway Rehabilitation Project                                      October 12, 1955
(T                    Sustainability
22.    The project sets out to: reduce NHA's dependence on the GOP for development and maintenance
financing through budgetary support; increase NHA's accountability with regard to its operations and
utilization of agency resources; strengthen in-country and NHA capacity to efficiently manage and operate
the system, and to maximize the development impact of limited resources; induct the Private Sector for
highway investments/operations wherever possible, to minimize the burden on the public exchequer, and
achieve policy changes/govermnent action needed to address other major bottlenecks to the development
of the sector. It will seek to reach agreement with government and other major donors in the sector, on a
carefully prioritized medium term investment program, that is consistent with the overall macroeconomic
framework, to ensure that counterpart funds are available in a timely manner. These are key steps aimed
at sustainable development and modernization of Pakistan's National Highway network.
Lessons from Previous Bank Assistance
23.    The Bank has had a long-standing relationship with NHA (and formerly NHB) covering several
operations spanning a number of years. Under Highways lV the Bank assisted NHB set up a maintenance
management system, which has been operating successfully for the last five years and has enabled NHA to
prioritize and cost its annual maintenance operations and to introduce "'maintenance by contract". Under
the Transport Sector Project, the Bank assisted in the reorganization of NHB into NHA, and helped
finance a major portion of the accumulated maintenance backlog on the national highway network. As a
result of past Bank operations and assistance, NHA is gradually evolving into a modern highway agency.
Significant progress was made on the institutional side in early 1995, when the National Highway Council
approved the revised NHA organizational structure and service rules. Regularization of "work-charge"
staff is now underway. The current projects are generally progressing well, and expected to close by mid
1996, having achieved a number of their major development objectives.
24.    Previous efforts at NHA institutional strengthening require continued support. However,
technical assistance in future projects needs to be more carefully focused, and rely more on local expertise
where available, to utilize and develop available in-country capacity and to achieve sustainable benefits.
Policy changes have traditionally been difficult to achieve during the course of project implementation,
and should be ensured by specific up-front action.
25.    Targeted resource mobilization measures were not explicitly included in previous highway
projects, with the result that broader fiscal constraints and the limited availability of counterpart funds (for
investments and maintenance) have adversely effected project objectives and agency performance. This
position was exacerbated when the borrower embarked on major highway expansion program during
1991-93. There is therefore a need to carefully design future projects within prevailing macro constraints,
and to reach binding agreement nith the borrower on the framework of an economically justifiable
medium term public sector investment program in the highway sector.
Rationale for Bank involvement
26.    Given the importance of an efficient transportation system for Pakistan's economy, and the
country's offorts to achieve export led growth, and the predominance of road transport in the present
inter-modal niix (for both passengers and freight), the project would strengthen the current thrust of the
Bank's operations in the highway sector, by enhancing resource mobilization, improving efficiency and
transparency in operations, introducing sustainable new arrangements for financing and managing
highway maintenance, promoting private sector participation and by assisting with highway upgrading in
the economically strategic, high density, Karachi-Lahore-Peshawar corridor.
- 5-



*_ _
Attachment-6
Page 7-of 8
Proposed Highw2y Rehabilitation Project                                       October 12, 1955
27.    Important initiatives are currently underway within NHA in a number of areas including: axle
load control, ROW preservation, development of BOT/PSP policies, development of a Pavement
Management System, a limited research program to tailor international pavement design methodologies
and material specifications to the Pakistani context, etc. Continued Bank assistance is needed to build on
the progress achieved to date. The proposed operation while financing upgrading of the highest priority
portions of the National Highway Network, and would encourage GOP in taking difficult decisions needed
to develop and sustain a modem and efficient highway system.
28.    It is proposed that the Bank leverage its resources in the sector, by inviting other co-financiers to
participate in the project.
Environmental Aspects
29.    Appropriate incentives to adopt measures which reduce environmental damage by vehicular
traffic will be incorporated in the revised structure of road user charges. Details would be developed
during project preparation.
30.    There would be very few resettlement issues involved, as the project would finance civil works
essentially -aithin the existing ROW corridors. Recycling of existing pavement materials in construction
of the new pavement sections would be explored to the maximum extent possible, to minimize adverse
environmental impact. Traffic in both directions would be restricted to the additional carraigeway during
the construction period.
Risks
31.    The following project risks can be identified at this stage:
a.     Delays in implementation of key policy actions.
b.      Changed political and/or sectoral priorities.
C.      Announcement of major new projectslinvestments in the sector, that are politically
driven, but of low economic priority.
d.     Inadequate availability of counterpart funds, Ieading to a delay in the completion of
ongoing NHA projects, and thereby delaying the start of new projects.
Timing and reporting
32.    Project processing is expected to follow the following schedule:
Identification mission:   Oct. 1995
IEPS                  Jan 1996
Appraisal:            Dec. 1996
Negotiations          Jun. 1997
Board presentation    Dec. 1997
Effectiveness         Jun. 1998
33.    While the project could be prepared with in the time frame indicated above, it is unclear whether
the borrower will be able to implement the up-front actions listed in para 12 within the required time
frame.

SECTION - I
1.1   HISTORY
The World Bank (The Intemational Bank of Reconstruction and Development) and the
National Highway Autlhoity (originally the National Highway Board) have enjoyed a close
association for3 decades. Commencing with theconstruction of theSuperHighwaybetween
Karachi and Hyderabad in the nineteen sixties the World Bank has assisted with the financing
of over 1,000 kilometres of highways in Pakistan, which represents 15 percent of the total
National Network.
Between 1964 and 1993 a total of US$  '1.5 m;ilion (does not include flood relief) in loans
have been sanctioned by IBRD 'or hi4w .ay development and rt-c.  istruction in Pakistan. Of
this amounlt US$ 295 million will have been utilized by Decendhei 1995. With Government
counterparL f Iding t'iis represents a total iivestment of about US$ 600 million or in terms
of 1993 Rupees about Rs 18 billion.
