In additioni to the Bank's ma jor r ole of assisting tlit-oughl loans to finance tlle direct costs of
higlhway constr-uction, the Worlld Bank has been instrumental in assisting the Governmentof
Pakistan in establislhing thle National Higlhway AuLhority, an autonomous organization under
the Ministry of Communiications whichi is entrusted with the planning, developmcnt,
maintenanice and oper-ation of PakisLanI's national highway network system.
The Goverinmncit of Pakistani neootiated the First Highway Project loan with the World Bank
durinig 1963. This initial World Bank loan was for US$ 17 million, which along with
cquivalcnt flulIds in Rupees l;rom the Government of Pakistan, was utilized to build the Super
Highway between Kaiaclii and Hyder-ahad, a distance of 120 kilometres and three major
bridges on the G T Road, N-5. The Super Highway, 7.3 meters (24 feet) wide with paved
shoulders was the first modem, high speed highway, to he constructed in Pakistan. The Super
Highway, built by the Italian contractor Astaldi, (after the firm had completed substantial
work of the Tarbela Dam) served with minimum maintenance, as a major, heavily trafficked
higlhway for l 8 years, wlen il 1986 the Highiway was overlaid to extend the useful life of the
high way, possibly for anotiher- 10 years.
A loan of US$ 35 million for the Second Highway Project was negotiated between the
Governmnent of Pakistan and the World Bank in 1969, immediately following the success of
the First IBRD Higlhway Project.
Piior to loan evaluation and negotiation, the Government had identified, subsequent to
feasibility studies and financial justification, two sections of highway to be reconstructed.
A section of the G T Road, N-5 from Sahiwal to Lahore and the Lahore-Sargodha-Mianwali
link.
Suibsequient to r-eceiving conltractor's bids for the two sections, the Government decided that'
thle costs werec too hiighi, and r-egr-ettably, thle Second IBRD Highway Project was cancelled
3



FHP Final Report
in June 1971. Negotiations for a loan for the Third IBRD Highway Project were commenced
almost a decade after the Second Highway Project had been cancelled and in 1982 a loan for
the amount of US$ 5( million was finalized for the Third IBRD Highway ProjecL
The Thir d IB RD Highway Proiect consisted of the reconstruction of three major sections of
the G T Road, N-5. Rohfi to Kotdiji, 45 kms, Khairabad to Nowshera, 29 kms, Peshawar to
Xl         Charsadha, 27 kms and Lahore to Sahiwal, 150 kms. Construction work, awarded to local
construction companies was commenced in 1982 and substantially completed 4 years later
in 1986.
Under the Third IBRD Highway Project a total of 251 kilometres of 7.3 meter w ide, asphalt
concrete paved highway with hard shoulders was successfullycomrpleted at atot. Icostofless
than US$ 80 million of wlhich 50 percent was provided under the World Bankloan agreement
anld 5 percentby the Governmentof Pakistan. In terms of 1993 Rupees thecostperkilometre
of constuLiction was approximately Rs 5 million per kilometre.
Idenltilication & appraisal by the Wol-id 3ank for a Fourth IBRD Highway Project com-
menced in November 1984. Loan negotiations between the Government of Pakisran and the
World Bank for a US$ 152 million loan were concluded in early 1987.

1.2 PROJECT IDENTIFICATION AND DEVELOPMENT
The pro ject was identified by the National Highway Board in co-operation with the Planning
Division and the National Transport Research Centre (NTRC).
The initial project concept, which consisted of four separate components was the subject of
numerous meetings between personnel of the National Highway Board and the World Bank
during 1985 and 1986. These discussions cumulated initially in the publication of the PC-
I followed shor tly by the publication of the World Bank Staff Appraisal Report during early
1987. The PC-I (original and revised) is given as Annexure "A" and the World Bank Staff
Appraisal Report (SAR) is given as Annexure "B". During the latcr palt of the above
desc:ibed prroject development, the consultants, STV Lyons and Associates were engaged by
the National Highway Board to oversee the Technical Development of the project. The STV
Lyon's conisultancy was fitnanced tlhrough thle Technical Assistance component of the Third
IBRD Higlhway Project.
The NHB also awarded six design consultancies to local consultants in order to prepare the
(lesign, constructioin drawings and bid documents for the sections of highway selected to be
inCludLC(I in the project. The fees of these consultancies were also covered by the Third
Higlhway Project ftunds. Tile loan for Fourth IBRD Highway Pro.ject No. 2814, for US$ 152
imillion (US Dollars one hudlldred and fifty tuA.) million only) was negotiated between the
World Ban,k and tlie Gover-:inment of Pakistan (Economic Affairs Division, Minir!ry of
Finance (EAD)) durinc, mitcd 1987 anld was signedand became effective 10 September 1987.

1.3  PROJECT COMPONENTS
The Fourth IBRD Highway Pro ject was planned primarily as a project to address dualization
and/or reconstruclion of selected sections of the N-5 National Higlhway. The major portion
of the planned Finances were assigned to the costs of civil works. However the project did
have three other components as detailed in the PC-l and SAR.
The four major project components are:
1. Civil Works                     The dualization of the N-5 and other associated con-
struction works.
2. Maintenance of
National Highwvays              Technical assistance for the development and imple-
mentation of a Maintenance System for National High-
ways
3. Higlhway Safety                  Develop a Pilot Highway Patrol on a section of the N-5
and implementation of a Driving Instructor's Training
Schiool.
4. Assistance to tlhe
Provinces (Balochiistan)        Technical Assistance to the provincial Communica-
tions and Works Departments.

1.4   PROJECT OBJECTIVES
The prinicipal ob jectives of thle Fourth IBRD Highway Project were:
(a)   ti lower transportation costs on the Karachi-Peshawar N-5, National Highway
by road rehabilitation (Civil Works);
(b)   to develop thie capacity of the National Highway Board (National Highway
Authiority) to plaii and nianage lhiglhway maintenance and operation, and to
introduce modern techinical and rinancial management practices;
(c)   to improve traffic safety on Nationial Highways;
(d)   to develop the capacity of Pakistani contractors to execute mainitenance and
rehabilitation wvorks using modern techniques, equipment and materials;
(e)   to continue to mobilise revenues from  road users for the maintenance of
National Higihwvays.
1.5   POST PROJECT REVIEW OF PC-1
The Project PC-I developed by tlhe National Highway Board during 1986-87 (attached to this
report as Annex "A") has ser-ved thl-oughiout the project as a useful guide in establishing and
mainitaininig the various suhi-ogalnizations within the NHA responsible for carrying out the
various tasks associated witl the project. In so far as the ma jorcomponent of the pro ct, Civil
Works (construction) was conceriled the PC-i has proved to be an extremely useful
document throughlout the pro ject. and has been extensively referred to as the "Engineer's
Estimates".
The otlher project components. Higlhway Safety and Assistance to Balochistan are weekly
portrayed and the Maintenance component was given negligible input.
As the oflicial GOP project docume1t, the PC-I was used by management as a basis forcost
and schedule control. It is a gr-eat pity that the authors of the PC- I and the authors of the World
Bank Staff Appraisal Report did not liaise more closely during the preparation of these
documents. Thcre ar e some substantial diffeirences in the concept and estimated costs of the

project componenits whicih were a continuous source of mis-understanding throughout the
project. The overall estimated cost of theproject as reported in the PC-1 was Rs 4,800 million,
whilst the SAR estimates the overall project cost at Rs 5,039 million. Both documents were
developed using the same exchanlge rates and both the same project components.
The PC- I was revised, as per GOP procedures during the later part of 1994. The revised PC-
I is given in Annexure "A".

1.6   POST PROJECT REVIEW OF SAR
The World Bank Staff Appraisal Report (SAR) for the Fourth IBRD Highway Project,
published on 14 April 1987, by the Transport Division, South Asia Projects Development,
has proved, throughout the project to be a valuable reference document.
The SAR is attached to this repor t as Annexure "B".
The SAR gives a clear and concise description of the project, with relevant backup
informationl and statistical analysis. The SAR gives a clear statement of the project's
ob jectives (whichi is not found in any other document related to this project) and detailedwrite -,
ups are given of lhow these objectives may be achieved.
The historical developmenit of the project is well documented and serves as'a permanent
record for lutuie projects in esLimating time frames from identification to implementation.
The SAR gives estimated project costs and individual project component costs. Both of
whichi have provenl extremely reliable thioughout the life of the project.
The SAR lias beeni extensively used by NH A management throughout the implementation
of lhe project as a valuable and reliable souice of project infoi.nation.

1.8  THE WORLD BANK LOAN AGREEMENT
Loan agzreemlient No. 2814 PK for an amount of US$ 152 million was signed between the 1
Goverinmenit ol Pakistan an( tlhe World Bank (lB 3LD) on 10 September 1987. A copy of the
loan agreemenit is given as Annexure "C" to lhis report.    ir,
In addition to the normal terms and conditionls of the loan such as: loan withdrawal policies,
ptocuLemenit ol services and goods, etc., lhere are several special "covenants" to the loan
agrcement which are discussed below, iciuLding their impact on the project.
1.       Special Account
An allowance was madh by the World Bank to deposit US$ 7 million, from loan funds
inlto a Special Accotint to be opetate(l by the National Highway Authority.
The puipose of tlhe Special Account or Revolving Fund was to reduce delays in
payineit time to conitractors. The eligiible portion of civil works payments were made
dir-ectly from the Special Accounl to the concerned contractor or consultant. Noti-
f'icationl of lthe paymeint, Logctliier with acknowledgement of the payment were then
forwarded to the World Batik who riepatr-iaLed the paid amount directly to the Special
Accoutlt, simultan.eously notilyitig the NHA of the amount drawn from the loan.
Because of tile incteased amouLits of disbursemenits frora the loan due to the higher
;   disbu-semnenLtratio for civil workLs .of0()I Septeinber 1990, the Special Account was
hincrascu to a float ol US$ 9 million.
10



FHP Final Report  !
Generally the Special Accounitsystem or Revolving Fund has operated satisfactorily
d during the lile ol the pro-ject. lnitially there were some delays caused by the banking
,.                    laws in Pakistan, whereby all foreign currency transactions passed through the State
Bank of Pakistan.
4.
In accordanlce witlih hc agreement, the World Bank commenced reduction of the
advance account in Junle 1994, anlicipating the loan closing date of 31 December
1994.
Due to some ouLstanding civil works and by mutual agreement, the loan closing date
was extende(d to 31 December 1995.
2.      Employment of Consultants
As acovenalnt Lto the loan it is sLipulated that the NHB would engage suitably qualified
and( experienced consultants to assist the NHB in cariying out the project.
The pur-pose of these consultants, as expanded upon in the SAR, would be to provide
techinical assistance lor project management, technical assistance for planning,
progr-amminng and execution ol maintenance and supervisory consultants for thecivil
wor-ks contr-acts.
The NHB complied witli tlie covenanit an(d by late 1987, early 1988, agreements had
beeni signed and consulLanils for Pro jcct Management, Maintenance and Supervision
ha(d becn mobilized.
The technical seivices and super-vision scrvices carried out by the consultants has
henil hiighily beileficiazl to thie NHl A.

3.      Construction Industry

commitLee to review import pirocedures pertaining to construction machinery, spare

The comnmitLee was to repoi-t its 1indinlgs by 01 July 1988 to the World Bank. The
covenanl was complied wiLh and a te;port was duly submitted to the Bank.

The 300 km section ol the N- 5 hetw&en Lahorc and Islamabad is the most heavily travelled
anid has tie higihest denisity of traffic of any iiter-ciLy link in Pakistan. On an average, during
1990-9 t tlalffic c6uLnts indicated thlal  10,000 vehicles per day used this section of highway.

ol this sccLioni of higlhway had traffic couInts in excess of 12,000 and individual sections
recoirdedi coLiits of 15,00() vceiicles per day. From these counts, it is apparent that loaded
Lrucks costitute aboutL 55 percent of all tiaffic. Thus if the Liaffic counts are converted to
ESA, the ligurie ol 20,00() ESAS is niot unireasonable.
Appiroximiiately 9() peicent of all stonle aggregate used in thle Punjab for construction
puiiposes coes roin the Taxila arei and is trlcked as far soutl as Sahiwal. It is estimated
lhat soine 20,000 tOnIS of cLiUSlC stone aggregate is transpor-ted down this section of the N-

3.2    DEVELOPMENT OF THE MAINTENANCE SYSTEM
A huclgct ol Rs 175 million was apIroved ror the maintenance improvement programme.

8.2    DISCUSSION OF PROJECT ACHIEVEMENTS
The achiievements of thle objectives ol' the Fourth IBRD Highway Project have been very
positive to the overall National Highway Network and have not been limited to the absolute
physical improvemenit of those sections of liighway which were constructed.
At the start up of the Fourth Highway Project, the Nalional Highway Board was located in
halfa dozen ol'fices over a bakcry in oHc of Islamabad's mark-ets. The Board was headed by
a Director General and had an overall annual budget of just over Rs 1.0 billion. At the time
the ongoilng developmCent projects werie limnited to 2 or 3 projects, the largest being the
Hydcrabad bypass with an estirnatcd construction cost of approximately Rs 300 million.
The adven t ther efore of the Fourthl Highway Project with a potential budget of around Rs 5.0
billion requir-ed more planning, administiation, technical inputs and management than the
entire NaLional Higlhway Board. The National Highway Board and the FHP becarne
integrated asonle. AstheFHPconi,stituted abouL9OpeicentoftheBoard'sworkload, theFHP
systems autom atically became thL  NI-B's systems.
Tihe World Bank had lor some time advocated a strong autonomous body capable of
planniiing, ceveloping, op,elating andc mailntaining the National Highways and to this end the
various technlical assistance availed to the NHB lor lile Fourth IBRD Highway Project, was
instrumental in assisting Llhe MiinistLy of Communications in establishing the National
Higlhway Authiotity.
Thle iniLial organization structure for the NiiA togethler with the proposed Policies and
Plrocediries were developed diniiig tlic Foutiilh Higlhway Project and implemented during the
3rd and 4Lt1 year of ltle projeci.
This was fortunlate because durinlg 199 1 the Government of Pak-istan embarked on a massive
higlhway ConlStrUCLiOnl programme, awarding bids betwcen late 1991 and mid 1992 of around
Rs 60 billion (inicludes Lahore-Islamahad Motorway).
The annual development budget was increased to around Rs 20 billion, but later trimmed
hack to about Rs II billion, at wlhiclh level iL has remainied during the past 3 years.
The institLutionial grounld worEk laid dullring tlhC Four th Highway Project was instrumental in
enabling the newly formned NFIA in dealing witlh this tremendously increased work load.
The metamor plhosis of the NHB to thle N1-A may definitely be attributed to the organizational
streng-tleninlg and the Policies and Procedur-es developed through the Fourth Highway
Project.

8.3.3  GOVERNMENT OF PAKISTAN FUNDS
As a condition of the loan the Government contributed to the project a portion of the cost
of the civil works.
Initially and as given in Schedule I of the loan agreement, the proceeds from the loan would
cover 51 percent of the cost of civil works and the Government of Pakistan would cover
the remaining 49 percent.
All other categories of the loan were financed 100 percent from proceeds of the loan.
At time of signing the Government's commitment to the project was approximately Rs
2,000 million which represented 49 percent of the cost of planned civil works. As of 01
September 1990, due to the Gulf War the disbursement ratio was changed to 70 percent cost
of civil works to be paid from loan proceeds and 30 percent the Government of Pakistan.
This condition prevailed up to and including 31 December 1994, at which time disburse-
ments reverted to the original 51 percent to 49 percent ratio.
The impact on the project of the changed disbursement ratio was initially to decrease the
overall project budget as demonstrated by the following equation:
(a)    Civil Works to be financed by the Loan at 51%:                                   US$  120.5 million
Equivalent 1987 Rupees:                                                       Rs 2,048.0 million
GOP Contribution at 49%:                                                      Rs 1.968.0 million
Total Available for Civil Works:                                     Rs 4,016.0 million

LESSONS LEARNT
Overall thc Projcct is judged a success, however ovcr the eight years of project
implementation, several issues were identified which had severe impacts on the project.
This section is therefore dedicated to identifying those issues and giving
recommendations for avoiding such pitfalls in future projects of this nature. It will be
noted that of the issues which are raised and of the recommendations given, there are
some which are directed at the World Bank project management procedures such
comments where given, are intended to be objective, bearing in mind that, firstly the
NHB at the inception of the project wds not equipped nor suitably staffed to handle
such a project and secondly the various Task Managers assigned by the World Bank,
attempted to counter this situation by "over-involvement" both technically and:
administratively.
